The rule of addition (al-idafah) in the principles of tafsir, principles of hadith, and other principles of sciences. When some usulis like the author of al-Tawdih took 'usul' added to 'fiqh' to mean 'rules', the author refuted him and said: 'Whoever takes al-asl (singular of usul) to mean a rule has erred' and neglected this known principle that when 'asl' is added to a science, it means the proof, because the rules of a science are its very issues, not its preliminaries. The gist of the refutation is that whoever takes 'asl' here to mean 'rule' like the author of al-Tawdih in the title definition of usul al-fiqh has erred in two ways: First, his neglect of this well-known principle that 'asl' when added to a science means 'proof'. Second, that 'usul' in its addition to a science, if taken to mean 'rules', would corrupt the meaning because the rules of a science are its very issues, not its proofs and preliminaries. Yes, if what is meant by 'asl' (whose plural is 'usul') is the lexical meaning—that which something else is built upon, which is general encompassing both proof and rule—then it may be taken as proof, thus 'usul al-fiqh' means its proofs, and it may be taken as the usuli rule upon which the particulars of usul al-fiqh are built. For this reason the author defined it as a title: 'It is a science of rules by which the mujtahid arrives at deriving fiqh rulings.' Then this science, usul al-fiqh, are general proofs for fiqh needed when applying detailed proofs to their indicated rulings. This statement indicates an explanation of the connection between fiqh and its usul, and also alludes to the appropriateness between the lexical meaning (from which it is transferred) of 'asl' and its terminological meaning (to which it is transferred), which is 'proof', meaning that fiqh is built upon its usul just as the indicated (ruling) is built upon its proof, because usul al-fiqh are general proofs for fiqh. Thus it is known that the proofs of fiqh are of two types: detailed proofs, like particular verses, hadiths, ijma, and qiyas; and general proofs which the detailed proofs need in applying them to their indicated rulings, and these are the rules of usul al-fiqh. A practical example of the need for general proofs in applying detailed proofs is our statement: 'Zakat is obligatory because Allah says 'and give zakat', for the command indicates obligation.' The example is in the form of a conjunctive syllogism: Zakat is commanded, and everything commanded is obligatory, so zakat is obligatory. The proof of the minor premise is the verse 'and give zakat', and the proof of the major premise is the usuli rule: that the command indicates obligation. And in the form of an exceptional syllogism: If zakat is commanded then it is obligatory, but it is commanded, so it is obligatory. The proof of the antecedent is the verse mentioned, and the proof of the implication is the statement 'the command indicates obligation', which is a usuli rule. So the ruling we need to know is the obligation of zakat (for example), the detailed proof for it is the verse 'and give zakat', and the general proof for it is the usuli rule that the command in this verse indicates obligation. And here is another linguistic rule (morphological): that 'atu' in 'watu al-zakata' is an imperative form. So if we say 'Zakat is obligatory' (this is a fiqh ruling), we support it with the detailed proof, the verse 'and give zakat', then we use the linguistic rule and say 'atu is a command' as a minor premise, then we attach to it the usuli rule and say 'the command indicates obligation' as the major premise, so the result is that zakat is obligatory. The linguistic rule that 'atu' is a command is also from the rules of usul, because usul discusses command and prohibition and their rulings. So it is better to say that...
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion clarifying the meaning of 'usul al-fiqh' and correcting a common error. The author begins by stating the general linguistic principle: when the word 'asl' (singular of 'usul') is added to a science, it means 'proof' (dalil), not 'rule' (qa'idah). He criticizes those who take 'usul' in 'usul al-fiqh' to mean 'rules', such as the author of al-Tawdih. The refutation is twofold: first, the established usage of 'asl' in idafah (construct phrase) with a science is for 'proof'; second, if it meant 'rules', it would be redundant because the rules of a science are its very issues (masa'il), not its proofs. However, the author acknowledges that in lexical sense, 'asl' can include both proof and rule. The terminological definition by the author of this text is that usul al-fiqh is 'a science of rules by which the mujtahid derives fiqh rulings'. Then he explains the relationship: usul al-fiqh are general proofs (adillah ijmaaliyah) that are needed when applying detailed proofs (adillah tafseeliyah) to the rulings they indicate. He illustrates with the example of deducing the obligation of zakat from the verse 'And give zakat' using the usuli rule that 'the command indicates obligation'. The example is presented as both a conjunctive syllogism and an exceptional syllogism. Finally, he notes that even a linguistic rule (like 'the imperative form indicates a command') is part of usul al-fiqh because usul discusses commands and prohibitions.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-asl): When added to a science, it means the proof (dalil) for that science.
أصول الفقه (usul al-fiqh): The science of general proofs (adillah ijmaaliyah) by which mujtahids derive fiqh rulings from detailed proofs.
الأدلة الإجمالية (al-adillah al-ijmaaliyah): General proofs or usuli rules (e.g., 'command indicates obligation') used to interpret and apply detailed proofs.
الأدلة التفصيلية (al-adillah al-tafseeliyah): Detailed proofs from Quran, Hadith, Ijma, and Qiyas that contain specific rulings.
الأمر للوجوب (al-amr li al-wujub): A usuli rule: the imperative form indicates obligation unless there is a qarinah (indication) to the contrary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Rule (qa'idah)
B) Proof (dalil)
C) Issue (mas'alah)
D) Foundation (asl)

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh are the general proofs (adillah ijmaaliyah) that guide the mujtahid in applying detailed proofs to derive fiqh rulings.

Understanding Detailed Proof: Philosophical, Logical, and Fiqhi Contexts
Distinguishing detailed proof in philosophy from logic and fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the subject matters of philosophical and logical detailed proofs and apply the concept to identify the subject of a fiqh detailed proof.

Core Idea
A detailed proof's subject varies: philosophy uses external objects, logic uses secondary intelligibles, and fiqh uses textual commands.

This passage clarifies the nature of 'detailed proof' (al-dalil al-tafsili) across disciplines. In philosophy, proofs deal with external existents, while logic concerns secondary intelligibles. The author illustrates with the example of proving the celestial sphere's shape and compares it to the Quranic command 'Give zakat', which is a detailed proof for the obligation of zakat. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to identify the proper domain of evidence in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify Definitions
Recognize the definitions of primary and secondary intelligibles and the respective subjects of philosophy (external existents) and logic (secondary intelligibles).
Primary intelligibles are concepts like 'human' or 'sun' that directly represent objects; secondary intelligibles are mental attributes like 'universality'.
2
Analyze the Philosophical Example
Break down the proof that the celestial sphere is spherical: it has a single nature, is a body, and every body with a single nature is spherical. Note that the subject (the celestial sphere) is a primary intelligible, not a logical concept.
The proof's subject is an external object, fitting philosophy's domain.
3
Compare with the Fiqhi Example
Consider 'Give zakat' (آتوا الزكوة) as a detailed proof for the obligation of zakat. Identify the subject: the command itself, which is a textual (linguistic) object, falling under fiqh and usul al-fiqh.
This command is an instance of a detailed proof in fiqh, analogous to the philosophical proof.
4
Apply the Distinction
Reflect on why a philosophical proof cannot serve as a logical proof: the subject of logic (secondary intelligibles) differs fundamentally from that of philosophy (primary intelligibles). This clarifies the boundaries of each discipline.
This distinction is crucial for proper classification of evidences in usul al-fiqh.

المثال التطبيقى : قولنا : "الفلك كروى الشكل مسألة من مسائل الفلسفة، ودليلها التفصيلى قولنا : لأن الفلك ذو طبيعة واحدة، لأنه جسم وكل جسم يكون ذا طبيعة واحدة فهو كروى الشكل، ينتج أنّ الفلك كروى الشكل، فالدليل التفصيلي لهذه المسألة الفلسفية قولنا : لأن الفلك ذو طبيعة واحدة، وهي صغرى الدليل، كما أنّ آتوا الزكوة دليل تفصيلى لمسألة فقهية، وهي أن الزكوة واجبة، وكانت الآية دليل الصغرى (لأنها مأمور بها).

And fire, earth, and air. So the detailed proof for a philosophical issue is not an individual of the subject of the science of logic, because the subject of the proof in philosophy is external existents, while the subject of the science of logic is secondary intelligibles and mental matters.
Applied example: Our statement: 'The celestial sphere is spherical' is an issue of philosophy, and its detailed proof is our statement: 'Because the celestial sphere has a single nature, because it is a body, and every body that has a single nature is spherical', resulting in that the celestial sphere is spherical. So the detailed proof for this philosophical issue is our statement: 'Because the celestial sphere has a single nature', which is the minor premise of the proof, just as 'Give the zakat' is a detailed proof for a juristic issue (i.e., that zakat is obligatory), and the verse is the proof of the minor premise (because it is commanded).
And the detailed proof for the philosophical issue (that the celestial sphere has a single nature) in terms of its matter and form is not an individual of the subject of the science of logic, because the subject of the science of logic is either a secondary intelligible or an individual of it or an essential accident of it (i.e., the subject of logic is either a universal or a species of it, like 'human', or an essential accident of it, like 'laughter' for human). The celestial sphere in the mentioned proof is none of these, rather it is from primary intelligibles like 'human' and 'Zayd'. So how can the subject of a philosophical proof be an individual of the subject of the science of logic? You will hardly find this except in this dictation. So take this and be among the grateful.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the nature of detailed proof (al-dalil al-tafsili) and its subject matter. The author distinguishes between philosophical and logical proofs. In philosophy, the proof's subject is external objects (al-mawjudat al-kharijiyya) like fire, earth, air, and the celestial sphere. In logic, the subject is secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniyya) and mental matters. The author gives an example: proving the celestial sphere is spherical uses a philosophical argument based on its single nature, which is a primary intelligible, not a logical one. Logic deals with universals and their accidents, not concrete entities like the celestial sphere. The comparison to fiqh shows that a Quranic verse (e.g., 'Give zakat') is a detailed proof for a juristic ruling, and here the ayah is the minor premise. The passage clarifies that the subject matter of logic and philosophy differ fundamentally.

Key Terms
الدليل التفصيلي (al-dalil al-tafsili): The detailed proof that connects a ruling to its subject, comprising premises leading to a conclusion.
المعقولات الأولية (al-ma'qulat al-awwaliyya): Primary intelligibles: concepts that directly represent external objects (e.g., man, Zayd, sun).
المعقولات الثانوية (al-ma'qulat al-thaniyya): Secondary intelligibles: mental concepts that apply to primary intelligibles (e.g., universality, genus, species).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Secondary intelligibles
B) External objects (primary intelligibles)
C) Linguistic constructs
D) Universal accidents

Key Takeaway
The subject matter of a detailed proof determines its disciplinary domain: philosophical proofs deal with external existents, logical proofs with secondary intelligibles, and fiqh proofs with textual commands.

The Fifth Definition of Fiqh and Its Restrictions
Explaining the restrictions in the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three restrictions in the Hanafi definition of fiqh and evaluate why each exclusion is necessary.

Core Idea
Fiqh is branch-related Shar'i wisdom: it excludes theology, non-Shar'i practice, and requires ijtihad.

In this passage, Shaykh Muhammad Anwar al-Badakhshani explains the technical definition of fiqh as 'branch-related Shar'i wisdom.' He unpacks each restriction, showing how they exclude 'ilm al-kalam (theology), non-Shar'i practical knowledge like crafts, and the knowledge of a muqallid. He also critiques a later innovation that restricts fiqh to sensory actions to exclude tasawwuf, affirming that such a restriction has no basis among the early scholars.

Guided Path
1
State the definition and its three components
Write down the definition: 'Fiqh is branch-related Shar'i wisdom.' Note the three key terms: ḥikma (wisdom), far'iyya (branch-related), shar'iyya (Shar'i).
2
Analyze the term ḥikma linguistically and technically
Examine the Qur'anic usage of ḥikma to see how it implies firm knowledge leading to action. The commentator explains that ḥikma here refers to a scientific faculty (malaka) acquired through ijtihad, not mere knowledge.
The distinction between ḥikma as faculty vs. knowledge is critical for excluding muqallid.
3
Understand the restrictions far'iyya and shar'iyya
Far'iyya excludes 'ilm al-kalam (theology) because it deals with original principles, not branch rulings. Shar'iyya excludes any practical wisdom not derived from the four proofs (e.g., crafts, Greek philosophy).
These restrictions maintain fiqh's focus on derived legal rulings.
4
Evaluate the requirement of ijtihad and the rejection of the sensory restriction
Recognize that the muqallid is not a faqih because he lacks the faculty of ijtihad. Then, learn why the later innovation of restricting fiqh to sensory actions (to exclude tasawwuf) is rejected as baseless among early scholars.
The only valid exclusion is through far'iyya and shar'iyya, not a new restriction.

وفي الاصطلاح هو حكمة فرعية شرعية.
٢- الحكمة في اللغة : اتقان العلم والعمل ، وقيل : هي العلم الراسخ الذي لا يحتمل الخطأ .
وفي الجلالين للسيوطي ص : ٤٢ ) تحت تفسير قوله تعالى يؤتِ الحكمة من يشاء )) ( هي ( العلم النافع المؤدى إلى العمل .
وذكر الله تعالى في سورة بنى اسرائيل ستة من النواهي وثلاثة من الأوامر (كلها يتعلق بالأحكام الفرعية وسمي الله كلها حكمة ، وقال : ذلك مما أوحى اليك ربك من الحكمة .
فعلم واعتقادية، فالفقه حكمة أي علم راسخ مع العمل به، و يتعلق بفروعات الشريعة الإسلامية ، وليس من الحكمة اليونانية التي اخترعها العقول، وخصصوا موضوعها بالموجودات الخارجية؛ إذ الفقه الأسلامى أعلى وأرفع من تلك الحكمة، وأهم موضوعاتها الاعتقادات الحقة والأخلاق الوجدانية.
أن الحكمة الأصلية هي الأحكام الشرعية فرعية كانت أو أصلية
والمراد بالعلم المفهوم من الحكمة الملكة العلمية الحاصلة من بذل الطاقة في حصول المسائل الفقهية ومن هنا لا يطلق الفقيه على المقلد ؛ لأن الفقيه من له ملكة فقهية، والمقلّد من لم يكن له ذلك، فكيف يقال : للمقلّد فقيه ؟
٣- و فرعية : معناها يتفرع وجوبها والعمل بها بعد وجوب الإيمان وقبول الأصول (أي العقائد الصحيحة الثابتة بالنصوص) وكذا يتفرع التكليف بالفرعيات بعد التكليف بالعقائد والأصول التي سماها المتقدمون من العلماء علم الكلام، فعلم الكلام حكمة أصلية، لا يتفرع على شيء آخر
ومعنى شرعية: أي تدل على ثبوت تلك الفروع والعمل بها الأدلة الشرعية (الأربعة التي يبحث عنها في أصول الفقه).
فالحكمة جنس تشمل الحكمة النظرية التي غايتها النظر والفكر دون العمل كعلم الكلام، والحكمة العملية التي المطلوب منها العمل، كعلم الفقه، فبقيد "فرعية خرج علم الكلام، وبقيد شرعية خرجت الحكمية العملية التي ليس لها تعلق بالشرع كالصنائع والحرف الجديدة، أي الحكمة الجديدة.
٥- ولا يطلق الفقيه على المقلد لتقصيره عن الطاقة أى طاقة الاجتهاد المشروطة للفقيه المجتهد، ومن أجل اعتبار الملكة العلمية في الفقيه (المفهومة) من لفظ خرج فقه المقلّد عن تعريف الفقه، وخرج المقلد نفسه عن تعريف الفقيه "الحكمة" المعتبر فيه وجود طاقة الاجتهاد.
والتخصيص بالحسبات احترازاً عن التصوف في تعريف الفقه حديث محدث، وكلام جديد لم يكن هذا التخصيص في المتقدمين نعم الاحتراز عن الكلام عرف معروف
والتخصيص (تخصيص الفقه بالحسيات (كأعمال الجوارح احترازاً (وحفاظا) عن (دخول) التصوف في تعريف الفقه حديث محدث وكلام جديد لم يكن هذا التخصيص في المتقدمين، وقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في تعريف الإحسان : " أن تعبد الله كأنك تراه " بيان للإخلاص في العمل، وليس فيه إشارة الى أي علم ،نعم الاحتراز عن (علم) الكلام (بقولهم: فرعية ) عرف معروف (عند المتقدمين فلا حرج في إخراجه عن تعريف علم الفقه )
والحاصل أن التقييد بقيد الحسيات" لإخراج التصوف(علم الأخلاق) عن تعريف علم الفقه أمر مبتدع لا أصله له عند المتقدمين .
The fifth definition in its commentary: he corrected the first definition, gave it preponderance, refuted the three definitions, and remained silent about the fifth definition. Explanation of the restrictions: 1. Fiqh linguistically: understanding and comprehension, as in the verse: 'They almost do not understand a word' and the verse 'O Shu'ayb, we do not understand much of what you say.' Terminologically: it is branch-related, Shar'i wisdom. 2. Al-Ḥikma linguistically: mastering knowledge and action, and it is said: it is the firmly rooted knowledge that does not admit error. In Tafsir al-Jalalayn (p. 42) under the interpretation of the verse 'He grants wisdom to whom He wills': it is beneficial knowledge that leads to action. Allah mentioned in Surah Bani Isra'il six prohibitions and three commands, all related to branch rulings, and Allah called all of them wisdom, and He said: 'That is from the wisdom your Lord has revealed to you.' So it is knowledge and beliefs; thus fiqh is wisdom, i.e., firmly rooted knowledge with action, and it pertains to the branches of Islamic law, and it is not of the Greek wisdom invented by minds, whose subject matter they restricted to external existents; for Islamic fiqh is higher and loftier than that wisdom, and its most important subjects are true beliefs and heartfelt morals. The original wisdom is the Shar'i rulings, whether branch or original. What is meant by the knowledge understood from wisdom is the scientific faculty (malaka) resulting from expending effort in acquiring fiqh issues. Hence, the term faqih is not applied to a muqallid, because the faqih is one who possesses the fiqh faculty, while the muqallid is one who does not have it. So how can the muqallid be called a faqih? 3. 'Branch-related' (far'iyya): meaning that its obligation and acting upon it branch off from the obligation of faith and acceptance of the principles (i.e., the correct beliefs established by texts). Similarly, the obligation of branch rulings branches off from the obligation of beliefs and principles, which the earlier scholars called 'ilm al-kalam. Thus 'ilm al-kalam is original wisdom, not branching from anything else. The meaning of 'Shar'i' (shar'iyya): it indicates that those branches and acting upon them are established by the Shar'i proofs (the four that are investigated in usul al-fiqh). So al-ḥikma is a genus that includes theoretical wisdom, whose goal is speculation and thought without action, like 'ilm al-kalam, and practical wisdom, from which action is sought, like fiqh. By the restriction 'branch-related', 'ilm al-kalam is excluded; by 'Shar'i', practical wisdom that has no connection to the Law, such as crafts and new professions, i.e., new wisdom, is excluded. 5. The term faqih is not applied to the muqallid due to his deficiency in energy, i.e., the energy of ijtihad required for the mujtahid faqih. Because of the consideration of the scientific faculty in the faqih (understood from the word), the fiqh of the muqallid is excluded from the definition of fiqh, and the muqallid himself is excluded from the definition of the faqih. The restricting to sensory matters (ḥissiyyāt) as a safeguard against including taṣawwuf in the definition of fiqh is a newly invented speech and a novel statement; this restriction was not present among the earlier scholars. Yes, the safeguard against 'ilm al-kalam by their saying 'branch-related' is a known practice among the earlier scholars, so there is no harm in excluding it from the definition of the science of fiqh. The conclusion: the restriction by the condition of 'sensory matters' to exclude taṣawwuf (the science of ethics) from the definition of the science of fiqh is an innovation with no basis among the earlier scholars.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage contains the commentary on the fifth definition of fiqh from the matn 'Musallam al-Thubut'. The author, Shaykh Muhammad Anwar al-Badakhshani, begins by noting that the original author corrected and preferred the first definition while refuting three others, and remained silent on the fifth. He then explains the restrictions in the definition 'fiqh is branch-related Shar'i wisdom'. Linguistically, fiqh means understanding, and ḥikma means mastering knowledge and action, or firm knowledge. The definition uses 'wisdom' because in the Qur'an, Allah calls His rulings wisdom. However, this wisdom is not Greek philosophy; it is specifically Shar'i knowledge pertaining to branches. The restriction 'branch-related' (far'iyya) excludes 'ilm al-kalam (theology), which is original wisdom and not branch-dependent. The restriction 'Shar'i' excludes any practical wisdom not connected to the Law, like crafts. The term 'wisdom' here implies a scientific faculty (malaka) acquired by expending effort; therefore the muqallid (imitator) who lacks this faculty is not called a faqih. Finally, the author addresses a later innovation: restricting fiqh to sensory (physical) actions to exclude taṣawwuf (spiritual/moral knowledge). He argues that this restriction has no basis among the early scholars; it is a novel invention. The early scholars only excluded 'ilm al-kalam via the 'branch-related' restriction, not taṣawwuf.

Key Terms
الحكمة (al-ḥikma): Firmly rooted knowledge that leads to action, specifically the Shar'i rulings (whether branch or original).
الفرعية (al-far'iyya): Rulings that branch off from the obligation of faith and acceptance of principles (beliefs).
الشرعية (al-shar'iyya): Rulings established by the four Shar'i proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas).
الفقيه (al-faqih): One who possesses the scientific faculty (malaka) of fiqh through expending effort and is capable of ijtihad.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows without having the faculty of ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a muqallid not called a faqih?
A) Because the muqallid does not have knowledge of the rulings.
B) Because the muqallid lacks the scientific faculty (malaka) acquired by expending effort for ijtihad.
C) Because the muqallid follows a different madhhab.
D) Because the muqallid does not act upon his knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh is precisely restricted: it is branch-related Shar'i wisdom, which excludes theology, non-Shar'i knowledge, and requires ijtihad capacity.

Defending the Definition of Fiqh as 'Ilm: The Hanafi Response to the Certainty Objection
Defense of the definition of fiqh against the objection based on the nature of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of 'ilm as including zann to defend the definition of fiqh, and distinguish the Hanafi view from the mutakallimun's restriction of 'ilm to certainty.

Core Idea
Fiqh is 'ilm because 'ilm includes zann; the mufta bihi definition uses 'ilm in its broader sense.

This passage from al-Badakhshani's commentary on Musallam al-Thubut addresses a critical objection to defining fiqh as 'ilm: if fiqh deals with zanni rulings, how can it be called knowledge? The Hanafi response reveals a fundamental divergence in the definition of 'ilm itself. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the epistemological basis of Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
The first objection concerns the necessity of action being a concomitant not part of the definition; the second objection denies that fiqh qualifies as 'ilm because it involves zann.
Note that the first objection is about the definition's form, the second about its content.
2
Isolate the Hanafi response to the first objection
The first answer: the definition is 'ilm bi-wujub al-'amal, which is qat'i, not zanni, thus fiqh as a discipline is certain. This refutes the second objection as well, though the author provides a separate second answer.
This answer relies on the definition being a definition by description (rasm), not essence.
3
Analyze the second answer: expansion of 'ilm
The second answer: 'ilm includes conceptualization and four types of assent (tasdiq), including zann, so defining fiqh as 'ilm is valid even if its subject matter is zanni.
The Hanafi usage of 'ilm here is broader than the mutakallimun's.
4
Contrast with the mutakallimun position
The mutakallimun restrict 'ilm to yaqin (certainty), so they would reject the definition. This contrast highlights the ikhtilaf.
This is a legitimate disagreement in terminology.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis can call fiqh 'ilm, while others may object; it affects how rulings are epistemologically classified, but not the rulings themselves.
This ikhtilaf is mainly definitional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المصنف في شرح قوله : " وفيه ما فيه : ان العلم بوجوب العمل وجود العلم بالأحكام، وليس من لوازم ماهيته، وإنما يكون المحمول من لوازم الماهية وليس وجوب العمل داخلاً في ماهية ،الفقه وإلا لم يكن حد علم الفقه بدون ذكر العمل، فلا يكون العلم بوجوب العمل مع كونه لازماً) معرفاً لعلم الفقه ولما أورد على تعريف الفقه إشكال آخر أشار المصنف إليه وإلى الجواب عنه بقوله: و من ههنا ( من أجل أنّ المراد من الأدلة الأمارات والأدلة الظنية، وأن الفقه عبارة عن العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام وهو قطعى علمت اندفاع ماقيل في الاعتراض: إن الفقه من باب الظنون فيكف يكون علماً ؛ ثمّ أشار إلى الجواب الآخر) و قال : على أنّ العلم (يستعمل) حقيقة فيما ( في علم) ليس بتصوّر أيضاً وهذا هو الإشكال الثاني الوارد على التعريف الثاني للفقه، وأجاب عنه المصنف أيضاً بوجهين: -١ تفصيل الإشكال : هو أن تعريف الفقه بالعلم غير صحيح؛ حيث أن الفقه ليس بعلم أصلاً ؛ فإن العلم في اصطلاح المتكلمين عبارة عن الاعتقاد الجازم الثابت المطابق للواقع ونفس الأمر، والفقه ليس كذلك ؛ فإنه عبارة عن العلم بالمسائل الظنية لا الاعتقاد الجازم، فلا يكون علماً، فلا يصح تعريفه بالعلم. -۲- وتفصيل الجواب الأول: أنّ الفقه هو العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام، وهو قطعي، وليس بظني ، و هذا التعريف تعريف بالرسم كما مر -٣ وتفصيل الجواب الثاني: أنّ العلم (كم) يطلق على التصور حقيقة يطلق علي غير التصور (أي التصديق بأنواعه الأربعة أيضا حقيقة، فيشمل العلم الأنواع الأربعة للتصديق : وهي الظن والجهل المركب والتقليد واليقين، فالمراد بالعلم المذكور في تعريف الفقه هو الظنّ بقرينة الأمارات المرادة من الأدلة).
The author said in commentary on his statement: 'And in it there is what is in it: that knowledge of the necessity of action is the existence of knowledge of rulings, and it is not among the necessary concomitants of its essence, but rather the predicate would be from the necessary concomitants of the essence, and the necessity of action is not included in the essence of fiqh, otherwise the definition of the science of fiqh would not be without mentioning action, so knowledge of the necessity of action while it is a concomitant is not defining the science of fiqh.' And when another objection was raised against the definition of fiqh, the author indicated it and the answer to it by saying: 'And from here (because what is meant by proofs are indicators and zanni proofs, and fiqh is the knowledge of the necessity of acting upon rulings, and it is certain, you know that what was said in objection is repelled: that fiqh is from the realm of conjectures, so how can it be knowledge?) Then he indicated the other answer and said: 'Rather, knowledge is used literally in that which is not conceptualization as well.' This is the second objection raised against the second definition of fiqh, and the author answered it also in two ways: 1- Detail of the objection: that defining fiqh as knowledge is invalid, because fiqh is not knowledge at all; for knowledge in the terminology of the mutakallimun is firm, stable belief corresponding to reality and the actual state of affairs, and fiqh is not like that; for it is knowledge of zanni issues, not firm belief, so it is not knowledge, thus its definition as knowledge is invalid. 2- Detail of the first answer: that fiqh is knowledge of the necessity of acting upon rulings, and it is certain, not zanni, and this definition is a definition by description (rasm) as has passed. 3- Detail of the second answer: that knowledge is applied literally to conceptualization, and it is also applied literally to non-conceptualization (i.e., assent in its four types), so knowledge includes the four types of assent: conjecture (zann), compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), taqlid, and certainty (yaqin). So what is meant by 'knowledge' mentioned in the definition of fiqh is zann, by the indication of the indicators meant by proofs.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the definition of fiqh as 'ilm (knowledge). The author (al-Badakhshani) is commenting on the matn of 'Musallam al-Thubut'. The discussion begins with the objection that if fiqh is defined as knowledge of the necessity of action, then that knowledge is a concomitant of fiqh but not part of its essence. Then a second objection is raised: fiqh deals with zanni issues, so it cannot be called 'ilm (which requires certainty). The author provides two answers: First, the definition refers to the knowledge of the necessity of action, which is certain (qat'i), so the objection is invalid. Second, even if we accept that fiqh involves zann, the term 'ilm is used literally for all four types of tasdiq (assent), including zann, jahl murakkab, taqlid, and yaqin. Therefore, defining fiqh as 'ilm is valid because 'ilm includes zann. This reflects the Hanafi approach to acknowledging zanni knowledge in fiqh, unlike the mutakallimun who restrict 'ilm to yaqin.

Key Terms
العلم (al-'ilm): In this passage, 'ilm is defined as including both conceptualization (tasawwur) and the four types of assent (tasdiq): zann, jahl murakkab, taqlid, and yaqin.
الظن (al-zann): Conjecture, a type of assent that is not certain but predominant opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Fiqh is based on hadith; and fiqh is practical.
C) Fiqh is the same as Qur'an; and fiqh is divine.
D) Fiqh is only about worship; and fiqh is limited.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends the definition of fiqh as 'ilm by broadening 'ilm to include zann, preserving the scientific status of fiqh.

Why Fiqh Cannot Be Limited to Certain Rulings
Criticizing the definition of fiqh as certain rulings with istinbat faculty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of certain rulings with istinbat' and explain why zanni rulings are included in the Hanafi concept of fiqh.

Core Idea
Defining fiqh as only certain rulings excludes most of the discipline.

In this passage, our author critiques a definition of fiqh proposed by the author of al-Tawdih (Sadr al-Shariah). That definition restricts fiqh to rulings that are certain (qat'i) and requires a faculty of istinbat. Our author objects that most fiqh rulings are not certain but zanni (based on khabar wahid and qiyas). Accepting that definition would leave only a small fraction of what we call fiqh. This lesson walks through the critique and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
First, find the exact definition attributed to Sahib al-Tawdih: fiqh is the knowledge of certain rulings (al-ahkam al-qat'iyya) together with the faculty of istinbat (malaka).
2
State the objection
The critic argues that this definition necessarily excludes all rulings established by zanni proofs (adilla zanniyya). Why? Because 'certain rulings' only includes those from qat'i proofs.
Zanni proofs include khabar wahid and qiyas, which are central to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the counter-argument
Some defenders of the definition claim that zanni rulings are few, so their exclusion is harmless. The critic refutes this by counting: khabar wahid and qiyas produce many rulings, while mutawatir reports are very few.
The critic emphasizes the quantitative mismatch.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
The critic concludes that the definition imposes an unwarranted restriction without proof. A proper definition of fiqh must include both qat'i and zanni rulings.
This aligns with the Hanafi position that fiqh encompasses all practical rulings, regardless of epistemological certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بعضهم كصاحب التوضيح) جعل الفقه عبارة عن الأحكام القطعية ملكة مع ملكة الاستنباط أي قال : هو العلم بالأحكام القطعية مع الاستنباط، ثم ذكر الايراد على هذا التعريف، وقال : ويلزم عليه على (هذا التعريف) خروج المسائل الثابتة بالأدلة الظنية عن تعريف الفقه ثمّ أشار إلى ردّ من يقول إنّ المسائل الثابتة بالأدلة الظنية قليلة، فلا يضر خروجها عن التعريف، فقال رداً على هذا القائل : وهي المسائل الثابتة بالأدلة (الظنية كثيرة كالمسائل الثابتة بأخبار الآحاد ،والقياس، ثم نوّر دعواه وقال : ألا ترى أيّها المخاطب) أن السنّة المتواترة (المفيدة للقطع) قليلة جداً، ثمّ ردّ على صاحب التوضيح وقال : والتزام ذلك إخراج المسائل الظنية عن تعريف الفقه التزام إيجاب على نفسه من غير ذكر دليل يستلزم ذلك الاخراج
And some of them, like the author of al-Tawdih, made fiqh consist of certain rulings with a faculty of istinbat. That is, he said: it is knowledge of certain rulings with istinbat. Then he mentioned the objection to this definition, and said: 'It necessarily follows from this definition the exclusion of issues established by zanni proofs from the definition of fiqh.' Then he indicated a response to one who says that issues established by zanni proofs are few, so their exclusion from the definition does not harm. He said in refutation of this speaker: 'And these issues established by zanni proofs are many, like issues established by khabar wahid and qiyas.' Then he clarified his claim and said: 'Do you not see, O addressee, that the mutawatir sunnah (which gives certainty) is very few?' Then he refuted the author of al-Tawdih and said: 'And committing to that — the exclusion of zanni issues from the definition of fiqh — is an imposition upon oneself without mentioning any proof that entails that exclusion.'
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the third definition of fiqh given by the author of al-Tawdih (likely Sadr al-Shariah). He defined fiqh as the knowledge of certain rulings along with the faculty of istinbat (derivation). The critic (our author) objects that this definition excludes all zanni rulings, which are a major part of fiqh. Some proponents of that definition argued that zanni rulings are few and their exclusion is negligible, but the critic refutes this by pointing out that zanni rulings from khabar wahid and qiyas are numerous, while mutawatir reports (which yield certainty) are very rare. Therefore, excluding zanni rulings would leave only a tiny portion of fiqh. The critic concludes that the definition imposes an unwarranted restriction without proof.

Key Terms
الأحكام القطعية (al-ahkam al-qat'iyya): Rulings established by definitive proofs (mutawatir, etc.) that yield certainty.
الملكة (al-malaka): A faculty or aptitude for istinbat (derivation of rulings).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of certain rulings with istinbat'?
A) Because most fiqh rulings are zanni, not qat'i.
B) Because istinbat is not a valid method.
C) Because certain rulings are impossible to know.
D) Because the definition includes too many issues.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh in the Hanafi school includes both qat'i and zanni rulings; any definition excluding zanni rulings is incomplete.

Rejecting Action from the Definition of Fiqh
Rejecting the inclusion of action in the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in al-Bazdawi's definition of fiqh as action and apply the Hanafi distinction between 'ilm and 'amal to evaluate definitions of codified sciences.

Core Idea
Action is the goal of fiqh, not its definition – 'ilm and 'amal are distinct categories.

Imam al-Bazdawi proposed that fiqh includes acting upon rulings, effectively defining fiqh as action. Our author, al-Badakhshani, rejects this as a misunderstanding of what a codified science ('ilm mudawwan) is. In this lesson, we will dissect his critique and understand the precise boundaries of the term 'ilm in the Hanafi usul tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target definition
Read al-Bazdawi's position: that fiqh is divided into three parts, the third being 'action upon it', implying fiqh is action.
Al-Bazdawi's definition is: 'Fiqh is acting upon the rulings.'
2
Locate the author's objection
Find the phrase 'بعيد جداً' (very far from correct) – this signals the rejection. The author argues that including action in the definition is 'ghayr masmu'' (not heard) from scholars.
The rejection is based on scholarly consensus of terminology.
3
Analyze the alternatives for codified science
The author lists three possibilities for what 'ilm mudawwan can mean: the issues themselves (al-masa'il), assent to them (al-tasdiq), or the faculty (al-malaka) from that assent. Action is not among them.
These are the standard Hanafi categories for the essence of a science.
4
Understand the author's positive construction
Action is the goal (ghaya) of knowledge and its intended purpose, but it is not the knowledge itself. The definition of a science must capture its essence, not its outcome.
This distinction preserves the purity of the definition.
5
Apply the distinction to other definitions
Consider how this critique would apply if someone defined fiqh as 'acting upon the shar'i proofs' – the same flaw would exist because action is external to the cognitive act of knowing.
The principle is general: 'amal is always the fruit, not the tree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإمام البزدوي لما قسم الفقه في أصوله إلى ثلاثة أقسام: قال والقسم الثالث هو العمل به حتى لايصير نفس العلم مقصوداً . فكأنه قال: الفقه هو العمل بالأحكام الشرعية، فردّ المصنف عليه وقال: وجعل العمل داخلاً في تحديد هذا العلم (تعريف الفقه) كما ذهب إليه بعض مشايخنا (وهو الامام البزدوى بعيد جداً لأن العلم المدوّن إما عبارة عن نفس المسائل، أو عبارة عن التصديق بها . أو عن الملكة الحاصلة من تصديق تلك المسائل . وأما إطلاق العلم على نفس العمل فغير مسموع عن القوم ، نعم يكون العمل غاية للعلم، ومقصود آمنه.
And Imam al-Bazdawi, when he divided fiqh in his Usul into three parts, said: 'And the third part is action upon it (i.e., the ruling), so that the knowledge itself is not the goal.' So it is as if he said: Fiqh is acting upon the rulings. The author refuted him and said: 'And making action included in the definition of this science (the definition of fiqh) as some of our shuyukh (i.e., Imam al-Bazdawi) held is very far from correct. Because the codified science (al-'ilm al-mudawwan) is either the issues themselves, or assent to them, or the faculty (malaka) resulting from assenting to those issues. As for applying the term 'ilm to the action itself, it is not heard from the people (scholars). Rather, action is the goal of knowledge and its intended purpose.'
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage criticizes the definition of fiqh by Imam al-Bazdawi, who divided fiqh into three parts and considered the third part to be 'action upon it'. This led to the implication that fiqh is action. The author (al-Badakhshani) rejects this, stating that the term 'ilm (knowledge) in a codified science ('ilm mudawwan) refers either to the issues themselves, or to the assent (tasdiq) to them, or to the faculty (malaka) arising from that assent. Applying 'ilm to action itself is unheard of among scholars. Action is the goal (ghaya) of knowledge and its intended purpose, but it is not the same as knowledge. Therefore, including action in the definition of fiqh is misguided.

Key Terms
العمل (al-'amal): Action; the critic argues it is the goal of 'ilm, not the definition.
الملكة (al-malaka): A faculty or skill, here derived from assent to rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject Imam al-Bazdawi's definition of fiqh as action?
A) Because action is not 'ilm according to scholarly usage.
B) Because action is part of 'ilm.
C) Because Bazdawi was not a Hanafi.
D) Because action is impossible.

Key Takeaway
A codified science is defined by its content (issues, assent, or faculty), not by its practical application; action is the goal, not the definition.

Defining Usul al-Fiqh as a Science of Principles
Defining usul al-fiqh as a science of principles for deriving rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to restate the definition of Usul al-Fiqh as a science of principles and demonstrate how a mujtahid uses such principles to derive a fiqh ruling from a textual proof.

Core Idea
Usul al-Fiqh is the science of principles (qawā‘id) that empower a mujtahid to derive fiqh rulings from their detailed proofs.

This lesson unpacks the technical definition of Usul al-Fiqh as a title (lqaban). You will see that this discipline is a systematic methodology, not merely a collection of rules. The passage uses the obligation of prayer from the Quranic verse 'Aqim al-salah' to illustrate how two usul principles—identifying the imperative form and interpreting it as indicating obligation—work together to produce a fiqh ruling. Understanding this definition is foundational to all subsequent usul study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key components of the definition
The definition states: 'a science of principles by which the mujtahid arrives at deriving fiqh rulings from their evidences.' Work out what 'principles' and 'derivation' refer to.
Principles are the methodological rules; derivation is the process of extracting rulings from sources.
2
Locate the illustrative example
The passage gives the obligation of prayer as a fiqh ruling derived from the detailed proof 'Aqim al-salah'. Identify the proof and the ruling.
The proof is the Quranic verse; the ruling is wujub (obligation).
3
Extract the two principles applied
The commentator shows that two usul principles are needed: first, that 'Aqim' is a command (amr); second, that the command indicates obligation (al-amr li al-wujub). State how each principle contributes.
The first principle categorizes the text; the second interprets its legal force.
4
Understand the dual meaning of 'usul'
The passage explains that 'usul' can mean either the four evidences (adillah) or the methodological principles (qawā‘id). It clarifies why the author chooses 'qawā‘id' in this definition.
Choosing 'qawā‘id' emphasizes that usul is a set of rules, not just the sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف البزدوى (التعريف الرابع ) ذكر إشكالاً واحداً، وأما التعريف الخامس الذي أشار إليه في التعليق فسكت عنه، لأنه تعريف المتقدمين، وشامل للعلوم الإسلامية كلها.

The definition of al-Bazdawi (the fourth definition) mentioned one problem. As for the fifth definition which he pointed to in the commentary, he remained silent about it because it is the definition of the early scholars and inclusive of all Islamic sciences.
Definition of Usul al-Fiqh as a Title:
As for its meaning as a title, it is a science of principles (qawā‘id) by which the mujtahid arrives at deriving fiqh rulings from their evidences.
Applied example: The obligation of prayer is a fiqh ruling. It can only be reached from its detailed proof (Establish prayer) with the help of two principles:
The first: that (Aqim) is a command.
The second: that the command indicates obligation. These are two usul principles.
And when Usul al-Fiqh is a title for a specific science, al-usul (plural of asl) in its linguistic meaning is that upon which something else is built. For the building of fiqh in its derivation from its evidences is based on specific principles that are researched in the science of Usul al-Fiqh, just as the establishment of its proof is based on one of the four evidences. From these two aspects, al-usul may mean the evidences as mentioned in the annex definition, and it may mean the principles, as the word (bi-qawā‘id) was chosen here instead of the word (bi-usul). The definition of qā‘idah and its applied example in the form of conjunctive syllogism and exceptional syllogism have already passed in the explanation of the preface, so there is no need for repetition.

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the definition of Usul al-Fiqh as a title (lqaban). It first notes that al-Bazdawi mentioned two definitions (fourth and fifth) but only discussed the fourth. The author then presents the standard definition: Usul al-Fiqh is the science of the principles (qawā‘id) that a mujtahid uses to derive fiqh rulings from their detailed evidences. To illustrate, he takes the obligation of prayer: it is derived from the Quranic verse 'Aqim al-salah' (Establish prayer) by applying two usul principles—first, that 'Aqim' is an imperative, and second, that the imperative form indicates obligation (this is the Hanafi position: al-amr li al-wujub). The passage then clarifies the term 'usul': it can refer to the evidences (adillah) or to the principles (qawā‘id), but in this definition, the author chooses 'qawā‘id' to emphasize the methodological rules. The reference to the earlier discussion of syllogistic forms indicates that this is a commentary building on prior material.

Key Terms
أصول الفقه (usul al-fiqh): A science of principles (qawā‘id) by which a mujtahid derives fiqh rulings from their detailed proofs.
القاعدة (al-qā‘idah): A principle or rule used in deriving rulings; here, 'the command indicates obligation' is given as an example.
الأمر (al-amr): The imperative form; in this context, it is taken to indicate obligation (wujūb).

Key Takeaway
Usul al-Fiqh is the science of principles that enables a mujtahid to transform textual evidence into fiqh rulings systematically.

Hadd vs Resm: Are Definitions of Sciences Essential or Descriptive?
Examining whether definitions of codified sciences are real (had) or descriptive (resm)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between hadd and resm, evaluate the argument that definitions of codified sciences are resm, and apply the author's refinement regarding iʻtibār.

Core Idea
Definitions of codified sciences are resm, not hadd, because sciences are conceptual composites without real genus and differentia.

In usul al-fiqh and other codified sciences, the definitions we encounter—such as 'fiqh is knowledge of practical legal rulings'—appear to describe the subject matter. But are these definitions truly capturing an essence (hadd) or merely providing a description (resm)? This passage from a commentary on Muslim al-Thubut tackles this question, presenting a proof that definitions of sciences are resm and then critically examining that proof. Understanding this debate sharpens our grasp of how the Hanafi tradition conceptualizes the nature of its own disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of definitions
Identify the two types: hadd (by essential properties) and resm (by accidental properties and concomitants). Note that hadd requires genuine genus and differentia.
2
Distinguish real vs conceptual quiddity
Real quiddities (e.g., animal) exist externally; conceptual quiddities (e.g., word, universal) exist mentally. Codified sciences belong to the conceptual category according to the majority.
3
Analyze the argument that sciences are resm
The argument: Sciences are composites of individual issues (like ten composed of units). These parts cannot be predicated of the whole, so the whole has no genus or differentia. Hence, definitions can only be resm.
The analogy of ten and units is key.
4
Evaluate the author's objection
The author objects that the difference between predicated and non-predicated parts is merely a matter of consideration (iʻtibār). A single thing can have multiple considerations, so multiplicity of essentials by consideration is not contradictory.
Refer to his work Sullam al-‘Ulum for elaboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن التعريف (عند المنطقيين) على نوعين:
حد إن كان بذاتيات المعزف، ورسم إن كان بعرضياته ولوازمه .
ثم الماهية (ما يقع في جواب السئوال بما هو في المذكر، والسئوال بما هي في المؤنث)
على نوعين:
حقيقية، كماهية الحيوان والنبات وغيرهما من الموجودات الخارجية، واعتبارية كماهية الكلمة والكلام، والكلي والجزئي والأصل والفرع، وغيرها من الموجودات الذهنية
و ماهيات العلوم المدونة من القسم الثاني عند الجمهور.
وهل للعلوم ( كالفقه وأصوله، والنحو والصرف والمنطق) ماهيات حقيقية؟ فيكون تعريفاتها حداً لها، أولها ماهيات اعتبارية؟ فتكون تعريفاتها رسمالها، فبدأ المصنف في هذا البحث وقال:
-
- قيل حقائق العلوم المدونة (هي) مسائلها المخصوصة أو التصديق بها فرداً فرداً، وأمّا المفهومات الكلية الداخلة في تعريفاتها التي تذكر في مقدمات الكتب فأجزاء محمولة عليها، (وهي) مسائلها المخصوصة أو إدراكاتها (أي التصديق بتلك المسائل

-٣ ثمّ اعترض المصنف على هذا الدليل بطلان تعدد الذاتي وقال: وفيه نظر أي) هو محلّ توجّه العقل وتفكره في صحته أشرت إليه في (السلّم) (سلّم العلوم وهو كتاب للمصنّف في المنطق)
وقال المصنف في تعليقه الذي عبر عنه ب (منه):
حاصل ما ذكر فيه (في السّلّم أن الفرق بين الأجزاء المحمولة وغير المحمولة انّما هو بالاعتبار، ولا يمتنع تعدد الاعتبارات لشيئ واحد بالذات فاللازم تعدد الذاتي بالاعتبار، وهو ليس بمحال؛ لأنّ العقل قد يعتبر أحدهما لاكليهما، وإنما المحال وجود ذاتين حقيقيين، فافهم
And know that definition (according to logicians) is of two types:
A hadd (definition) if it is by the essential properties of the thing being defined, and a resm (description) if it is by its accidental properties and necessary concomitants.
Then quiddity (that which occurs in answer to 'what is it' for masculine, and 'what is she' for feminine) is of two types:
Real, like the quiddity of animal, plant, and others from external existents; and conceptual, like the quiddity of word, speech, universal, particular, original, derivative, and others from mental existents.
And the quiddities of the codified sciences are from the second category according to the majority.
Do the sciences (such as fiqh, usul al-fiqh, grammar, morphology, and logic) have real quiddities, so that their definitions would be hadd for them? Or do they have conceptual quiddities, so that their definitions would be resm for them? The author began this research and said:
It was said: The realities of the codified sciences are their specific issues or the assenting to them individually. As for the universal concepts included in their definitions which are mentioned in the prefaces of books, they are parts predicated upon them. And they are their specific issues or their perceptions (i.e., the assenting to those issues).
Then he derived a branch from this statement and said: So the universal concepts mentioned in the prefaces (in the definitions of sciences for the sake of insight) are descriptions (rusūm) for them. And he provided a proof for this derivative and said: Based on the fact that a composite of parts that are not predicated (i.e., it is not valid to predicate them upon it) like ten (composed of units) has no genus and no differentia, i.e., such a composite has no genus and no differentia, otherwise it would entail multiplicity of the essential, i.e., this composite would have two essential properties.
Then the author objected to this proof regarding the impossibility of multiplicity of the essential and said: There is consideration in it (i.e., it is a place for the intellect to reflect on its validity; I have alluded to this in (al-Sullam) (Sullam al-‘Ulum, which is a book by the author in logic).
And the author said in his commentary which he expressed with (minhu):
The summary of what was mentioned there (in al-Sullam) is that the difference between predicated and non-predicated parts is only by consideration, and the multiplicity of considerations for one thing by essence is not impossible. So the necessary outcome is multiplicity of the essential by consideration, and that is not impossible, because the intellect may consider one without the other. The impossible is the existence of two real essences. So understand.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the discussion on definitions. It begins by explaining the logical classification of definitions: hadd (definition by essential properties) and resm (definition by accidental properties and necessary concomitants). It then introduces the concept of quiddity (māhiyyah), which is either real (such as animal and plant) or conceptual (such as word, universal, etc.). The author notes that according to the majority, the quiddities of codified sciences (like fiqh, usul, etc.) belong to the conceptual category. He then presents a view (qīl) that the realities of these sciences are their specific issues or the mental assenting to them, and that the universal concepts mentioned in their definitions are merely parts predicated upon them. Consequently, the definitions of sciences are only resm (descriptions), not hadd. The proof given is that composite entities whose parts cannot be predicated of the whole (like ten composed of units) have no genus and differentia; adding genus and differentia would lead to multiple essentials. The author then objects to this proof, arguing that the difference between predicated and non-predicated parts is merely a matter of consideration (i‘tibār), and a single thing can have multiple aspects, so having multiple essentials by consideration is not a contradiction. He refers to his own work in logic (Sullam al-‘Ulum) for elaboration. This discussion is relevant to usul al-fiqh because it affects the status of definitions of the discipline itself. The Hanafi position would align with the view that definitions of sciences are resm, as they are conceptual constructs.

Key Terms
الحدّ (al-hadd): A definition by essential properties (dhatiyyat).
الرسم (al-rasm): A description by accidental properties (aradiyyat) and necessary concomitants.
الماهية (al-māhiyyah): The quiddity; what is answered to 'what is it?'; either real or conceptual.
التصديق (al-tasdīq): Assent; believing a proposition to be true; here, it is one of the components of a science's reality.
الأجزاء المحمولة (al-ajzā’ al-mahmūlah): Parts that can be predicated of the subject (e.g., the concept 'body' is predicated of 'human').
الأجزاء غير المحمولة (al-ajzā’ ghayr al-mahmūlah): Parts that cannot be predicated of the subject (e.g., units are not predicated of ten).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are composed of universal concepts that cannot be predicated of their subject
B) Because they have no real genus and differentia, being composites of individual issues
C) Because they are based on revealed texts
D) Because the majority of scholars chose the term resm

Key Takeaway
Definitions of codified sciences are resm (descriptions), not hadd (essential definitions), because sciences are conceptual constructs composed of individual issues without real genus and differentia.

Resolving the Objection of Union of Conception and Assent in Defining Sciences
Critical refinement of a definitional objection in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the objection that defining sciences leads to union of conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq), and explain the Hanafi response using the distinction between mental form and external reality.

Core Idea
Definition is conception; if the defined involves assent, they would unite — resolved by taking 'science' as mental form, not external.

We have just seen that the earlier objection of multiple essential properties is resolved because one quiddity is external and one mental. However, a deeper problem emerges: if the concepts we use to define sciences are taken as actual definitions, they would force an impossible union between conception and assent. This lesson unpacks that objection and shows how a careful reading of 'science' in the context of the unity of knowledge and known avoids the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Recall the resolution of the first objection
Remember that having two essential properties is only invalid if both are external or both mental. Here, one is external (the composite) and one is mental (the definition), so no contradiction.
This sets the stage for the next objection.
2
Identify the new problem
Even if definitions are allowed, they cause union of conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq), which are distinct types of knowledge.
The author says: 'Yes, it necessitates the union of conception and assent.'
3
Understand the two interpretations of 'science'
The defined science can be either the propositions themselves (al-masā'il) or the assent to them (al-taṣdīq bihā).
Both lead to the same problem.
4
Follow the proof of union
If science is assent, then definition (a conception) is identical to the defined (assent). If science is propositions, then definition unites with propositions, and due to unity of knowledge and known, propositions unite with assent, so again conception unites with assent.
This is the core rational argument.
5
Examine the hint 'fatafakkar' (so reflect)
The resolution is that in the unity of knowledge and known, 'science' means mental form (al-ṣūrat al-ʿilmiyya), not external proposition or assent. So the definition (also a mental form) does not necessitate union with the external assent.
Thus the definitions remain permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شيئ ( في مرتبة الإطلاق وعدم اعتبار القيود وجوداً وعدماً) جزء ذهني للماهية المركبة كالحيوان (للإنسان فوجب حمله على ذلك المركب، وفي مرتبة بشرط لا شيئ (أي بشرط خلوه عن القيد يكون ذلك الجنس مادة ويمتنع حمله على ذلك المركب، وكذلك الفصل في مرتبة بشرط لا شيئ صورة، ويمتنع حملها على المركب المذكور، لأن ذلك المركب ليس عبارة عن المادة فقط أو عن الصورة فقط حتى يصح حملهما عليه واحدة واحدة، وإنما يجوز حملهما معاً، فلا يقال: الإنسان جسم فقط أو نفس فقط، وأما الأجزاء الذهنية فجاز حمل كل واحد منها منفرداً على الكلّ ، فيقال : الإنسان حيوان، كما يقال الإنسان ناطق .
فعلم أنّ الأجزاء الذهنية تستلزم الأجزاء الخارجية، فيكون للمركب الذهني حقيقتان : خارجية وذهنية، فثبت له ذاتيان . ولكن هذا ليس بباطل ؛ لأنه ليس بين الماهيتين (الخارجية والذهنية تناقض، بل يكون بينهما تلازم كما مر

نعم، يلزم اتحاد التصور والتصديق حقيقة، مع أنهما نوعان (متباثنان) تحقيقاً (حقيقة) (وأشار إلى وجه لزوم الإشكال أو إلى الجواب عنه
فقال: فتفكّر
وأشار في الحاشية التي كتب بعدها (منه) إلى تفصيل ذلك الإشكال، وقال: نعم يلزم الخ يعنى يلزم على تقدير كون تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم اتحاد التصور والتصديق ؛ لأن الحد عين المحدود وحقيقته، وهذا إذا كانت العلوم عبارة عن الادراكات فظاهر، وأما إذا كانت حقائقها نفس المسائل، فتلك بناء على اتحاد العلم والمعلوم، فتدبر (منه)
قوله : نعم يلزم اتحاد التصور والتصديق الخ: لما لم يصح الاعتراض السابق (لزوم تعدد الذاتي عند المصنف جاء باعتراض آخر ( على تقدير كون تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم ) أي إذا كانت تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم المدوّنة يلزم اتحاد التصور والتصديق وهو باطل، فكونها حدوداً أيضاً باطل، فقال المصنف في دليل بطلان اللازم (اتحاد التصور والتصديق مع أنهما نوعان (متباثنان) تحقيقا أي حقيقة، وأشار إلى بيان اللزوم بقوله (فتفكر).
وحاصل دليل اللزوم أنّ العلم المعرف إما عبارة عن نفس المسائل، أو عن التصديق بها، فعلى التقدير الثاني (كون العلم عبارة عن التصديق بالمسائل) لزوم الاتحاد بين التصور والتصديق ظاهر، لأنّ الحدّ عبارة عن تصور المحدود، والمحدود هو العلم الذى هو نفس التصديق، فلزم اتحاد التصور (الحد) واتحاد التصديق (المحدود) وعلى تقدير الأول (كون العلم نفس المسائل) يكون الحد متحداً مع العلم، وهو نفس المسائل، والمسائل متحدّة مع الادراكات المتعلقة بها؛ لأجل ضرورة الاتحاد بين العلم والمعلوم، فصار الحد متحداً مع التصديق المتعلّق بالمسائل، فلزم اتحاد التصور والتصديق على التقدير الأول أيضاً.
ويمكن أن يكون في قوله ( فتفكر) إشارة إلى اعتراض، وهو أن العلم في صورة اتحاد العلم والمعلوم يكون بمعنى الصورة العلمية، لا بمعناه الحقيقى (ما به الانكشاف لأنه (المصنف) قال في السّلّم : " إن العلم في مسألة الاتحاد بمعنى الصورة العلمية فالعلم بمعنى نفس المسائل أو التصديق بها لا يكون متحدّاً مع المعلوم، بل العلم بمعنى الصورة العلمية يكون متحداً مع المعلوم، ففي صورة كون تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم لا يلزم الاتحاد بين العلم والمعلوم، ولا بين التصور والتصديق، فبقى الجواز والإمكان تادا. (إمكان كون تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم)
A thing (at the level of absolute and non-consideration of restrictions in existence and non-existence) is a mental part of a composite essence, like 'animal' (for 'human'), so it must be predicated of that composite. And at the level 'with the condition of nothing' (i.e., with the condition of being free from restriction), that genus becomes matter, and its predication of that composite is impossible. Similarly, the differentia at the level 'with the condition of nothing' is form, and its predication of the mentioned composite is impossible, because that composite is not just matter or just form such that each could be predicated of it one by one; rather, both may be predicated together. So it is not said: 'Human is only body' or 'only soul'. As for mental parts, each one of them may be predicated individually of the whole, so it is said: 'Human is an animal', just as it is said: 'Human is rational.' Thus it is known that mental parts entail external parts, so the mental composite has two realities: external and mental, and thus it has two essential properties. But this is not invalid, because there is no contradiction between the two quiddities (external and mental); rather, there is a necessary connection between them as previously. Yes, if one thing has two external realities or two mental realities, this is invalid, because one would suffice for the other. So if those concepts mentioned in the definitions of sciences were a definition of them, the problem would not arise. And the conclusion of the consideration that he pointed to in al-Sullam and repeated due to its goodness in al-Musallam is that the multiplicity of the essential property or the existence of two realities for one thing is only invalid if they are both external or both mental. But if one is external and the other mental, there is no problem in it. So the objection falls away from those concepts being definitions of the codified sciences. But there remains here another problem that the author pointed to with his statement: 'Yes, it necessitates the union of conception and assent in reality, even though they are two distinct types actually (truly).' And he pointed to the reason for the problem or to the answer to it by saying: 'So reflect.' And he pointed out in the marginalia written after it (from him) the detail of that problem, saying: 'Yes, it necessitates...' etc., meaning: on the assumption that those concepts are definitions of the sciences, it necessitates the union of conception and assent, because the definition is the very thing defined and its reality. This is apparent if the sciences are the perceptions. But if their realities are the propositions themselves, that is based on the unity of knowledge and the known. So ponder (from him). His statement: 'Yes, it necessitates the union of conception and assent...' etc.: When the previous objection (the multiplicity of the essential property) was not valid according to the author, he came with another objection. (On the assumption that those concepts are definitions of the sciences) i.e., if those concepts are definitions of the codified sciences, it necessitates the union of conception and assent, and that is invalid. So their being definitions is also invalid. Thus the author said regarding the proof of the invalidity of the consequent (the union of conception and assent while they are two distinct types) actually i.e., truly, and he pointed to the explanation of the necessity by his statement 'So reflect.' The gist of the proof of necessity is that the defined knowledge is either the propositions themselves or the assent to them. On the second assumption (that knowledge is the assent to the propositions), the necessity of union between conception and assent is apparent because the definition is a conception of the defined, and the defined is knowledge which is itself the assent. Thus there is a union of conception (the definition) and assent (the defined). On the first assumption (that knowledge is the propositions themselves), the definition is united with knowledge, which is the propositions themselves, and the propositions are united with the perceptions related to them because of the necessity of union between knowledge and the known. Thus the definition becomes united with the assent related to the propositions. So the union of conception and assent follows on the first assumption as well. And it is possible that in his statement 'So reflect' there is an indication of an objection, which is that knowledge in the case of the unity of knowledge and the known is in the sense of the mental form, not in its true sense (that by which disclosure occurs). Because the author said in al-Sullam: 'Knowledge in the issue of unity is in the sense of the mental form.' So knowledge in the sense of the propositions themselves or the assent to them is not united with the known. Rather, knowledge in the sense of the mental form is united with the known. So in the case of those concepts being definitions of the sciences, there is no necessity of union between knowledge and the known, nor between conception and assent. Thus permissibility and possibility remain. (The possibility of those concepts being definitions of the sciences.)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from a super-commentary (hāshiya) on Musallam al-Thubūt. It deals with a philosophical objection concerning the definition of sciences. The author first resolves an earlier objection: that defining a science would require multiple essential properties (dhātiyyān) for one thing. He shows that this is only problematic if both quiddities (māhiyyāt) are external or both mental; if one is external and the other mental, there is no contradiction. This clears the way for the definitions to be used. However, a new and deeper objection arises: if these concepts are taken as definitions (ḥudūd) of the sciences, they lead to the union (ittiḥād) of conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq), which are two fundamentally distinct types of knowledge. The argument runs as follows: A definition is a conception of the defined thing. The defined here is 'science' (ʿilm). But 'science' can be understood in two ways: either as the assent to the propositions, or as the propositions themselves. If science is the assent, then the definition (a conception) would be identical to the defined (an assent), thus conflating the two types. If science is the propositions themselves, then due to the principle of the unity of knowledge and the known (ittiḥād al-ʿilm wa al-maʿlūm), the propositions are united with the perceptions (idrākāt) of them, and the definition (a conception) would be united with that assent, again leading to union. The author of the base text (al-muṣannif) signals a possible way out by saying 'fatafakkar' (so reflect). The commentator suggests that this reflection points to a distinction: knowledge in the sense of the unity of knowledge and the known is the mental form (al-ṣūrat al-ʿilmiyya), not the true meaning of knowledge (that by which disclosure occurs). When science is taken as the mental form, the definitions do not entail union, because the mental form is not identical to either the propositions or the assent. Thus the objection is neutralized, and it remains permissible for those concepts to serve as definitions of the sciences.

Key Terms
تصور (taṣawwur): Mental representation of an object without judgment (e.g., conceiving 'human').
تصديق (taṣdīq): Assent or judgment about the truth or falsity of a proposition (e.g., believing that humans are rational).
حد (ḥadd): A definition that states the quiddity of a thing, typically through genus and differentia.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key resolution to the objection that defining sciences leads to union of conception and assent?
A) The definition is not a conception but an assent.
B) The definition is a mental form, and in the unity-of-knowledge context, 'science' means mental form, so no union occurs.
C) The definition is external and the science is mental, so they are different.
D) The objection is invalid because conception and assent are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
The objection of union of conception and assent in defining sciences is resolved by understanding that 'science' in the definition refers to the mental form, not to external reality.

Why Names of Sciences Are Common Nouns
Resolving the objection that names of sciences are proper nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the difference between a universal term (kullī) and a proper noun (ʿalam shakhṣ), and apply the Hanafi definition to classify terms like fiqh.

Core Idea
A term is a common noun if it applies to its individuals, even if it does not apply to its parts.

This passage tackles a common misconception: because 'fiqh' does not apply to each individual mas'ala, some might think it is a proper noun. The author clarifies that universals apply to composite wholes, not parts, and thus remain common nouns. By understanding the distinction between kullī and juz'ī, you will avoid misclassifying terms in usul analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize the claim that names of sciences are proper nouns because they do not apply to each part separately.
The objector argues from the lack of predication on parts.
2
Understand the concept of kullī
A universal term (kullī) applies to its individuals (afrād) but not to its individual parts (ajzā').
The individuals are composite wholes, not the parts.
3
Apply the concept to examples
Analyze the examples of 'four', 'sikanjabin', 'house', and 'fiqh' to see that they behave the same way: they are common nouns despite not applying to parts.
None of these terms apply to their parts individually.
4
Recognize the preventer
The preventer of predication on parts is partiality (juz'iyya), not the agreement or difference of parts.
Both uniform parts (like four) and diverse parts (like sikanjabin) are equally blocked.
5
Conclude the classification
Since names of sciences behave like other common nouns (house, four), they are not proper nouns.
The objection is invalid; the universal's application to individuals is decisive.

Source Text

Removal of Terror from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut. Person, so how can fiqh and its likes be a person's name? The solution: that the universal meaning may be composed of parts of uniform (reality) like the four composed of four units, and may be composed of parts of different reality, like sikanjabin composed of pepper, vinegar (and honey). So the four and sikanjabin do not apply to their parts and are not from proper nouns, so it does not follow from the lack of predication on some parts of the personal parts that the names of the sciences are proper nouns. (1) His saying: for necessity, and the necessity is not present (here): This statement is a response to Sayyid Zahid al-Harawi that the claim of commonality of the genus is only due to the necessity of preventing declension or other, and the necessity is not present here, for the necessity of the commonality of the word 'Usama' for the genus is because it is non-declinable, and there is no cause for preventing declension except the verbal feminine, and it is a single cause, so another cause must be considered for the validity of preventing its declension, so commonality is considered as it is a proper noun for a specific type of animal, the predatory animal (the lion). As for the names of the sciences, this necessity does not exist, so there is no need to consider commonality. His saying: and the solution etc.: The summary of the objection: just as fiqh does not apply to each of its parts, i.e., to each issue, likewise the house does not apply to the shard only, or to the walls only, i.e., to each of its parts, and yet it is a common noun, and is not a proper noun. So how can the names of the sciences like fiqh, grammar, morphology and the like be proper nouns due to their lack of application to each issue? This is the meaning of his saying: 'so it does not follow from the lack of predication on some that they are proper nouns for persons.' The summary of the solution: that the universal (whether it is the names of the sciences or other) only applies to its individuals (like the collection of different issues present in different books) and like different houses, and 'four' applies to different things, and types of sikanjabin. Indeed, the word (fiqh) and the word (four) and the word (sikanjabin) and the word (house) each applies to its individuals, but none of them applies to its parts separately, i.e., to each individual part. The consideration in the predication of the universal is the individuals, not the parts. Whether the parts are uniform in reality, like the parts of the four, or different in reality like the parts of the house, and the parts of the sciences (their issues) and the parts of sikanjabin (pepper, vinegar, and honey). So the preventer of predication is partiality, not the agreement or difference of parts. So in preventing the predication of parts on the whole individually, the names of the sciences and other composites whose parts are not predicated are all equal, so they are also equal in not being proper nouns.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This is a usul definition passage that resolves an objection regarding whether the names of Islamic sciences (like fiqh, nahw, sarf) are proper nouns (a‘lam shakhsiyya) or common nouns (asmā’ jins). The objection arose because a term like 'fiqh' does not apply to each individual mas’ala (part) separately, which might suggest it is a proper noun referring to a specific entity. The author rebuts this by clarifying the nature of universal (kullī) terms. A universal term refers to a whole composed of parts; it is predicated on its individuals (afrād), which are the composite sets (e.g., all fiqh issues in various books), but not on the individual parts alone. The examples of 'four' (composed of four units) and 'sikanjabin' (a drink made of pepper, vinegar, and honey) illustrate that even composed terms do not apply to their parts individually. Similarly, 'house' does not apply to just a wall or a shard, yet it is a common noun. The preventer of predication on parts is partiality (juz’iyya), not the uniformity or diversity of parts. Therefore, the names of sciences remain common nouns, not proper nouns, despite not applying to each part separately. This passage is a classic example of how Hanafi usul analyzes linguistic terms to derive legal classifications.

Key Terms
كلي (kullī): A universal term that refers to a whole composed of parts, predicated on its individuals (afrād) but not on its individual parts (ajzā’).
علم شخص (ʿalam shakhṣ): A proper noun that refers to a specific individual entity, not a general class.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it applies to the set of all masā'il as an individual.
B) Because it is a technical term.
C) Because it is derived from a root verb.
D) Because it is applied by jurists.

Key Takeaway
A universal term (kullī) is classified as a common noun based on its predication of individuals, not its parts.

The Subject of Usul al-Fiqh: Four Sources Unified by Purpose
Defining the subject of Usul al-Fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh according to the Hanafi school and explain why the multiplicity of sources does not entail multiple sciences.

Core Idea
Usul al-Fiqh is one science because all four sources share one purpose: leading to a sharʿī ruling.

This passage defines the subject (mawḍūʿ) of Usul al-Fiqh. A common confusion is that since the field deals with multiple sources (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas), it must be multiple sciences. The Hanafi response clarifies that a single unifying function—īṣāl ilā ḥukm sharʿī—makes them one subject. This lesson unpacks the definition and the rebuttal, giving you the conceptual foundation for the entire science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of Usul al-Fiqh
Locate in the text what the author declares as the subject (mawḍūʿ) of the science. Note that it is the four sources considered 'in general' (ijmālan).
The author explicitly states: 'موضوعه الأدلة الأربعة'.
2
Recognize the objection
Understand the potential objection: if a science is defined by its subject, having four different subjects implies four different sciences. The text uses the analogy of medicine vs. logic.
The phrase 'وهم إيراد الإشكال' introduces this objection.
3
Find the unifying aspect
Locate the author's rebuttal: the four sources are unified by a common denominator—they all lead to (īṣāl) a sharʿī ruling and establish it.
The key phrase: 'الأدلّة الأربعة مشتركة في الايصال الى حكم شرعى'.
4
Distinguish general vs. detailed study
The passage clarifies that Usul al-Fiqh studies sources in a general sense (ijmālan); detailed verse-by-verse or hadith-by-hadith analysis belongs to Tafsir and Hadith.
This demarcation helps to avoid overlap with other sciences.
5
Note the three opinions on ḥujjiyya
The author reports three opinions regarding the classification of discussions on the authority (ḥujjiyya) of Ijma, Khabar al-Wāḥid, and Qiyas. The text mentions two fully and a third incomplete.
Opinion 1: belongs to Fiqh. Opinion 2: not a theoretical issue. Opinion 3 (incomplete): likely belongs to Usul al-Fiqh.
