موضوع أصول الفقه
وموضوعه (أصول الفقه) الأدلة الأربعة : (وهي الكتاب والسنة والاجماع والقياس إجمالاً، أي من حيث الإجمال، من غير بيان أنواع الكتاب والسنة والإجماع والقياس باعتبارات مختلفة وأما من حيث التفصيل آية آية وحديثاً حديثاً فإنما يبحث في علم التفسير والحديث ، وليس هذا البحث من علم الأصول.
ولما كان وهم إيراد الإشكال بأن تعدد الموضوع يدل على تعدد العلوم فيلزم منه أن يكون أصول الفقه أربعة علوم، فإن تمايز العلوم باعتبار تعدد وتمايز الموضوعات، فالطب والمنطق علمان مختلفان لاختلاف موضوعها، فأشار المصنف إلى ردّ هذا الوهم وقال:
(الأدلّة الأربعة مشتركة في الايصال الى حكم شرعى (واثباته)
وحاصل الكلام أن تعدد الموضوع يدل على تعدد العلوم إذا لم يكن بين تلك الامور المتعددة جهة الوحدة والقدر المشترك، والقدر المشترك أو جهة الوحدة بين الأدلة الأربعة هنا هو الإيصال إلى الحكم الشرعي واثباته .
واعلم أن المصنف ذكر بالنسبة إلى حجية الاجماع والقياس ثلاثة أقوال : الأول: أن بحث حجية الاجماع وخبر الواحد والقياس من مسائل الفقه .
والثاني: أنه ليس بمسألة نظرية حتى يحتاج إلى البحث عنها في علم من العلوم، بل
The subject of Usul al-Fiqh. And its subject (Usul al-Fiqh) is the four evidences: (the Book, the Sunnah, consensus, and analogy) in general, i.e., from the perspective of generality, without explaining the types of the Book, Sunnah, consensus, and analogy with different considerations. As for from the perspective of detail, verse by verse and hadith by hadith, then that is researched in the science of Tafsir and Hadith, and this research is not from the science of Usul. Since there was a misconception that the multiplicity of subject indicates multiple sciences, so it would follow that Usul al-Fiqh would be four sciences, because sciences are differentiated by the multiplicity and differentiation of subjects; medicine and logic are two different sciences due to the difference of their subjects. So the author indicated the rebuttal of this misconception and said: (The four evidences are shared in leading to a legal ruling (and its establishment). The summary of the discourse is that multiplicity of subject indicates multiple sciences if there is no unifying aspect and common denominator among those multiple matters. The common denominator or unifying aspect among the four evidences here is the leading to the legal ruling and its establishment. And know that the author mentioned regarding the authority of consensus and analogy three opinions: First: that the research on the authority of consensus, solitary report, and analogy is from the issues of Fiqh. Second: that it is not a theoretical issue needing research in any science, but
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This usul definition passage establishes the subject matter of Usul al-Fiqh. The author states that the subject (mawḍūʿ) of this science is the four sources (adilla) — the Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas — considered in a general sense (ijmālan), meaning as a whole class of evidence, not in their detailed particulars (tafṣīlan). The detailed study of each verse or hadith belongs to Tafsir and Hadith, not Usul. The passage then addresses a potential objection: if the subject consists of multiple things, the science should be multiple as well. The author rebuts by explaining that what makes a science unified is a single unifying aspect or common denominator. In this case, all four sources share the common function of leading to a shar‘ī ruling (īṣāl ilā ḥukm shar‘ī) and establishing it. Thus, the four sources form one subject under one unifying principle. Finally, the author reports three opinions regarding the classification of the discussion of the authority (ḥujjiyya) of Ijma, Khabar al-Wāḥid, and Qiyas: (1) it belongs to Fiqh, (2) it is not a theoretical issue at all (implying it is self-evident or not needing scholarly investigation), and (3 — though incomplete) likely that it belongs to Usul al-Fiqh. The passage shows careful demarcation of Usul al-Fiqh's scope within the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; that which the science primarily investigates.
إجمالاً (ijmālan): In a general sense, without going into detailed particulars.
الإيصال (al-īṣāl): The act of leading to or conveying; here, the function of the sources in leading to a sharʿī ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the single unifying aspect that makes Usul al-Fiqh one science despite having four sources?
A) All four sources are revealed.
B) All four sources lead to a shar'i ruling.
C) All four sources are agreed upon by scholars.
D) All four sources are mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Usul al-Fiqh is one science because its four sources share the single purpose of leading to a sharʿī ruling.

The Predicate of a Composite Does Not Apply to Its Parts
Explaining that the predicate of a composite whole does not apply to its individual parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the usul principle that the predicate of a composite whole does not apply to its parts, identify partiality (juz'iyya) as the preventer, and apply it to examples from fiqh.

Core Idea
The whole's predicate does not extend to its parts; partiality, not heterogeneity, is the bar.

This passage from the commentary on Muslim al-Thubut clarifies a foundational logical point essential for understanding definitions and categorizations in usul al-fiqh. You will learn why a name that applies to a composite whole cannot be applied to any of its parts individually, regardless of whether the parts are similar or different. This principle has direct implications for how we understand the predication of legal terms (e.g., 'prayer' applies to the whole act, not to individual movements).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Claim
Read the opening sentence: 'None of them applies to its parts individually.' This is the core principle. Note that it refers to the predicate of a composite whole.
2
Distinguish Individuals from Parts
The author clarifies that what is considered in the truthfulness of a universal is the individuals (afrad), not the parts (ajzaa). A part is not an individual of the universal that applies to the whole.
3
Examine the Examples
Look at the examples: homogeneous parts (units of four), heterogeneous parts (house, sciences, sikanjabin). The preventer is partiality (juz'iyya), not agreement or difference. Confirm that each example demonstrates the same rule.
4
Apply to Names of Sciences and Composites
The author extends the same logic: names of sciences like 'chemistry' do not apply to individual questions or reactions. Similarly, mixed substances like sikanjabin do not apply to single ingredients.
5
Recognize Relevance in Usul
This principle governs how definitions and categorizations work in legal theory. It prevents fragmenting composite acts (like salah) and ensures precise application of legal terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يصدق شيء منها على أجزائه منفردةً أي على كلّ جزء جزء منه، والمعتبر في صدق الكلى هو الأفراد، لا الأجزاء . سواء كانت الأجزاء متفقة الحقيقة، كأجزاء الأربعة أو مختلفة الحقيقة كأجزاء البيت، وأجزاء العلوم (مسائلها) وأجزاء السكنجبين (الفلفل والخل والعسل) فالمانع عن الحمل هو الجزئية لا اتفاق الأجزاء واختلافها، ففي منع حمل الأجزاء على الكلّ انفراداً أسماء العلوم وغيرها من المركب من الأجزاء غير المحمولة كلّها سواء، ففي عدم كونها أعلاماً شخصية أيضاً سواء.
None of them applies to its parts individually, i.e., to each part of it. And what is considered in the truthfulness of the universal is the individuals, not the parts, whether the parts are of the same reality, like the parts of four, or of different reality, like the parts of a house, the parts of sciences (their questions), and the parts of vinegar sauce (pepper, vinegar, and honey). So the preventer of predication is 'partiality,' not the agreement or difference of parts. Thus, in preventing the parts from being predicated on the whole individually, names of sciences and other composites of parts that are not all predicated are the same, and in not being personal proper names, they are also the same.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from the commentary expands on a point from the matn regarding the logical relationship between a composite whole and its parts. The author clarifies that a predicate (like a name or universal) that applies to the whole does not apply to any single part individually. For example, the number 'four' is a whole; its parts (the individual units) are not each 'four.' Similarly, 'house' does not apply to a door or wall alone, nor does 'chemistry' apply to a single reaction. The key point is that the impediment is the fact of being a part, not whether the parts are homogeneous or heterogeneous. The same logic holds for mixtures like a drink (sikanjabin) that combines pepper, vinegar, and honey: the name of the mixture does not apply to any single ingredient. This principle is relevant in usul for understanding definitions and categorizations.

Key Terms
الكلى (al-kullī): The universal or whole that can be predicated of many individuals, as opposed to its parts.
الجزئي (al-juz'ī): The particular or part, which is not predicated of the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, the term 'salat' refers to a composite of specific actions. Based on the principle in this lesson, which of the following statements is correct?
A) 'Salat' can be applied to ruku alone because ruku is a part of salat.
B) 'Salat' applies only to the complete act, not to any single part.
C) 'Salat' applies to each part because they are all wajib.
D) 'Salat' does not apply to the whole because it is composite.

Key Takeaway
The predicate of a composite whole is exclusively for the whole as a unit, not for any of its parts, due to the intrinsic nature of partiality.

The Place of Ijma' and Qiyas: Usul al-Fiqh or 'Ilm al-Kalam?
Explains the disciplinary classification of ijma' and qiyas authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the authority of ijma' and qiyas is a kalam rather than usul topic, and identify the reasons usul scholars discuss it nonetheless.

Core Idea
The authority of ijma' and qiyas is a theological issue, included in usul only due to widespread disagreement.

You might assume that establishing the authority (hujjiyya) of ijma' and qiyas is a core task of usul al-fiqh. This passage from Musallam al-Thubut reveals a subtle methodological truth: the question of whether these sources are authoritative at all belongs to theology ('ilm al-kalam). Usul scholars only treat it because of historical controversy. Understanding this classification sharpens your grasp of the boundaries between Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third opinion
The author states there is a third opinion: the authority of ijma' and qiyas is from 'ilm al-kalam. Recognize that this is a meta-methodological claim about the discipline to which the discussion belongs.
The first two opinions (not quoted) likely placed it in usul or elsewhere. This third view reclassifies it.
2
Understand the analogy to Quran and Sunnah
The text says 'like the authority of the Book and the Sunnah.' Just as proving that the Quran is from Allah and the Sunnah is revelation belongs to kalam, so too proving that ijma' and qiyas are authoritative is a theological, not jurisprudential, question.
3
Grasp why usulis discuss it anyway
The commentator explains: because of the extensive disagreement (ikhtilaf) among scholars regarding the authority of ijma' and qiyas, usul scholars mention it incidentally (tiba'an wa istitradan). There was no such disagreement about Quran and Sunnah, so their authority is not discussed in usul.
This shows that usul al-fiqh is not the primary place to prove hujjiyya; it is taken as given from kalam.
4
Distinguish primary vs. subsidiary discussion
In kalam, the topic is treated intentionally (qasdan wa asalan). In usul, it is treated as a subsidiary digression. This distinction prevents confusion about the scope of each discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثالث : وأشار المصنف إلى القول الثالث وحقيتها فقال: بل الحق أنه (كون الاجماع والقياس حجة) من مسائل) علم الكلام كحجية الكتاب والسنة. شرح القول الثالث : يعنى مسألة حجية الإجماع والقياس ليست من علم الأصول، بل الحق أنها من مسائل علم الكلام، كما أن حجية الكتاب والسنة من مسائل علم الكلام . وأما البحث عن حجيتها (حجية الإجماع والقياس) في علم الأصول فلما يأتي : لكن تعرّض الأصولي لحجيتهما (حجية الاجماع والقياس فقط، لأنهما كثر فيهما الشعب رفع الأصوات والاختلاف، وأمّا حجيتهما (حجية الكتاب والسنّة) فمتفق عليها عند الأمة. ولكن الأصوليين إنما تعرّضوا لحجية الاجماع والقياس لأجل الاختلاف الكثير وردّ الناس بعضهم بعضاً في حجيتها، فذكرت حجيتهما في علم الكلام أيضاً قصداً وأصالة، وفي علم الأصول تبعاً واستطراداً. وأما حجية الكتاب والسنة فمن الأمور الاتفاقية عند الأمة المسلمة، فلا حاجة إلى ذكر حجيتهما في علم الأصول.
The third opinion: The author pointed to the third opinion and its truth and said: Rather the truth is that (that ijma' and qiyas are proof) is from the issues of the science of kalam, like the authority of the Book and the Sunnah. Explanation of the third opinion: It means that the issue of the authority of ijma' and qiyas is not from usul al-fiqh, but rather the truth is that it is from the issues of the science of kalam, just as the authority of the Book and the Sunnah is from the issues of the science of kalam. As for the discussion of their authority (the authority of ijma' and qiyas) in usul al-fiqh, it is for what will come: But the usuli addresses their authority (the authority of ijma' and qiyas only, because the branches raised voices and disagreement increased concerning them. As for their authority (the authority of the Book and the Sunnah), it is agreed upon by the ummah. And the usulis only addressed the authority of ijma' and qiyas because of the much disagreement, and people refuting one another regarding their authority. So their authority was mentioned in the science of kalam also, intentionally and as a principal matter, and in usul al-fiqh as a subsidiary and digression. And as for the authority of the Book and the Sunnah, it is among the matters of agreement among the Muslim ummah, so there is no need to mention their authority in usul al-fiqh.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage explains the third opinion regarding the placement of the discussion on the authority of ijma' and qiyas. The author of the matn, Musallam al-Thubut, asserts that the truth is that the issue of whether ijma' and qiyas are proof belongs to 'ilm al-kalam (theology), just like the authority of the Quran and Sunnah. The commentator (Shaykh Muhammad Anwar al-Badakhshani) elaborates: the question of authority (hujjiyya) of these sources is not a topic of usul al-fiqh per se; rather, it is a theological issue. However, usul scholars discuss the authority of ijma' and qiyas (but not the Quran and Sunnah) because there was extensive disagreement and controversy among scholars regarding their validity. The authority of the Quran and Sunnah is universally accepted, so no need to prove it in usul. Thus, the discussion in usul is incidental and digressive (tiba'an wa istitradan), while in kalam it is primary.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijmā'): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the ummah after the death of the Prophet on a religious matter.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical deduction from an original case to a new case based on shared effective cause.
حجية (ḥujjiyya): The quality of being a proof or authoritative evidence in Islamic law.
علم الكلام (ʿilm al-kalām): Theology; the science that deals with matters of creed and the rational proofs for Islamic beliefs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do usul scholars discuss the authority of ijma' and qiyas despite it being a kalam topic?
A) Because they are proofs from the Quran and Sunnah.
B) Because there is widespread disagreement about their authority.
C) Because they are part of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh.
D) Because they are derived from the science of kalam.

Key Takeaway
The authority of ijma' and qiyas is a theological question; its inclusion in usul is a subsidiary digression due to historical disagreement.

Are Rulings Part of the Subject Matter of Usul al-Fiqh?
Discusses the dispute over including ahkam in usul's subject matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether rulings are part of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh and identify the purpose of mentioning rulings in usul works.

Core Idea
Rulings are not the subject matter of usul al-fiqh; they are mentioned only for depiction and classification.

One of the foundational questions in usul al-fiqh is: what exactly does this science study? Some scholars argue that rulings (ahkam) are part of the subject matter alongside evidence, while others restrict it to evidence alone. This passage from Musallam al-Thubut addresses this dispute and clarifies the Hanafi position that rulings are not the subject matter, but are discussed only for illustrative purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The passage asks whether rulings (ahkam) are included in the subject matter of usul al-fiqh. Note that the author points out there is disagreement on this point.
The term 'subject matter' (mawdu') refers to what the science primarily investigates.
2
State the author's position
The author declares 'the truth is no' – rulings are not part of the subject matter. He explains that the purpose of mentioning rulings in usul is 'taswir wa tanwi'' (depiction and diversification), i.e., to define and classify rulings so that they can be linked to types of evidence.
This is the Hanafi position upheld in the text.
3
Recognize the opposing view
Some scholars, notably Sadr al-Shariah in al-Tawdih and al-Taftazani in al-Talwih, argue that rulings are part of the subject matter. Their reasoning: the common factor between rulings and evidence is 'al-ithbat' (proof); evidence is subject matter as proof, and rulings are subject matter as things proven.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both views are from Hanafi scholars.
4
Understand the answer to the objection
An objector asks: if rulings are not subject matter, why does the author discuss them? The author gives two answers. The first (given in the passage) is that rulings are mentioned not as subject matter, but as a digression (istitrad) and for completion. The second answer is incomplete due to a page break.
The first answer emphasizes that every science discusses things outside its subject matter when needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل تكون الأحكام أيضا داخلة في موضوع علم أصول الفقه؟ فأشار المصنف إلى الاختلاف الواقع في هذه المسألة وقال : و في موضوعية الأحكام اختلاف ثم قضى بينهم و قال و الحق لا (أي الأحكام لا تكون موضوعاً لعلم الأصول) (۱) و انما الغرض من ذكر الأحكام في علم أصول الفقه التصوير والتنويع (أى تعريف الأحكام و بيان أنواعها) ليثبت أنواعها (أي أنواع الأحكام) بانواع الأدلة و أشار إلى الوجه الآخر وقال) وما من علم إلا ويذكر فيه الأشياء الأخر التي ليست من موضوع ذلك العلم استطراداً وتبعاً تتميماً لمعرفة الأحوال) و ترميماً (أي اصلاحاً للغاية المقصودة) قوله : وفي موضوعية الأحكام اختلاف ويمكن أن يكون المقصود من هذه العبارة ثلاثة أشياء : الأول : بيان الاختلاف في كون الأحكام موضوعاً لعلم أصول الفقه. والثاني : الإشارة إلى ما هو الحق عند المصنف من عدم موضوعية الأحكام لأصول الفقه، مع أنّ صدر الشريعة في التوضيح والتفتازانى فى التلويح “اختارا كون الأحكام موضوعاً لأصول الفقه مثل الأدلة والقدر المشترك بين الأحكام والأدلة هو الاثبات، فالأدلّة تكون موضوعاً من حيث كونها مثبتة لها، والأحكام من حيث كونها مثبتة بالأدلة. والثالث : دفع اعتراض موهوم يرد على المصنف بأنه لم ترك الأحكام ولم يقل: وموضوعه الأدلة والأحكام؟ وحاصل الدفع أن المصنف بصدد بيان الامور الاتفاقية، وموضوعية الأحكام مختلف فيها. ولما ورد الإشكال بأن الأحكام لما لم تكن موضوعاً لأصول الفقه عند المصنف فلم ذكر الأحكام في كتابه ؟ فأجاب عنه بوجهين: الأول : ليس ذكر بحث الأحكام في أصول الفقه لأجل أنها موضوع علم
Are rulings also included in the subject matter of the science of usul al-fiqh? The author pointed to the difference occurring in this issue and said: And regarding the subject-matter-ness of rulings, there is difference. Then he judged between them and said: And the truth is no (i.e., rulings are not subject matter for usul al-fiqh). (1) And the only purpose of mentioning rulings in usul al-fiqh is for depiction and diversification (i.e., defining rulings and explaining their types) so that their types (i.e., the types of rulings) are established by the types of evidence. And he pointed to the other aspect and said: And there is no science except that other things which are not from the subject matter of that science are mentioned in it, digressively and subordinately, for the completion of knowing the conditions and for the repair (i.e., correction of the intended goal). His statement: 'And regarding the subject-matter-ness of rulings, there is difference' — and it is possible that the intention of this expression is three things: First: Explanation of the difference in rulings being subject matter for usul al-fiqh. Second: Indication of what the author holds as true — that rulings are not the subject matter for usul al-fiqh, even though Sadr al-Shariah in al-Tawdih and al-Taftazani in al-Talwih chose that rulings are subject matter for usul al-fiqh, like evidence, and the common factor between rulings and evidence is proof; so evidence is subject matter in terms of it being proof for them, and rulings in terms of them being proven by evidence. Third: Repelling a supposed objection that could be directed at the author: Why did he leave out rulings and not say: 'Its subject matter is evidence and rulings'? The gist of the repelling is that the author is concerned with explaining the agreed matters, and the subject-matter-ness of rulings is disputed. And when the objection came that since rulings are not subject matter for usul al-fiqh according to the author, why did he mention rulings in his book? He answered it in two ways: First: mentioning the discussion of rulings in usul al-fiqh is not because they are the subject of the science...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage addresses whether rulings (ahkam) are part of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh. The author (Musallam al-Thubut) states that there is disagreement on this, and declares that the truth is that rulings are not the subject matter. The commentator explains that the purpose of mentioning rulings in usul is only for 'depiction and diversification' (taswir wa tanwi'), i.e., to define rulings and classify their types so that each type can be linked to its appropriate evidence. The commentator then unpacks the author's brief statement into three points: (1) clarifying the dispute, (2) stating the author's position that rulings are not subject matter (contrary to the views of Sadr al-Shariah in al-Tawdih and al-Taftazani in al-Talwih, who held that rulings are part of the subject matter), and (3) defending the author's omission of rulings from his definition of usul's subject by noting that he is focusing on agreed-upon matters, while subject-matter-ness of rulings is controversial. Finally, the author is asked: if rulings are not the subject, why does he discuss them? His answer begins: 'First: mentioning the discussion of rulings in usul al-fiqh is not because they are the subject of the science...' The passage breaks off here, but the incomplete answer likely explains that they are mentioned as a subsidiary or for completion.

Key Terms
الأحكام (al-aḥkām): The legal rulings (taklifi and wad'i) derived from the evidence in usul al-fiqh.
موضوع (mawḍūʿ): The subject matter of a science; what the science primarily investigates.
تصوير وتنويع (taṣwīr wa tanwīʿ): Depicting (defining) and diversifying (classifying into types) rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, are rulings (ahkam) part of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh?
A) Yes, they are the main subject.
B) No, they are not; they are only mentioned for depiction and diversification.
C) Yes and no; it is a disputed matter.
D) Only the types of rulings are subject matter.

Key Takeaway
Rulings are not the subject matter of usul al-fiṭq; they are discussed only for definition and classification, while the true subject is the evidence and methods of derivation.

Are Rulings Part of Usul al-Fiqh's Subject Matter?
Debating whether ahkam are part of usul al-fiqh's subject matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dispute over the subject matter of usul al-fiqh and explain why the Hanafi author treats rulings as incidental rather than central.

Core Idea
The subject matter of usul al-fiqh is evidence, not rulings; rulings are mentioned only incidentally.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a line from the matn that notes a dispute: are aḥkām (legal rulings) part of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh? Some leading Hanafi scholars include them, but the author of this matn sides with the majority view that the subject is adillah (evidence) alone. The passage also preempts an objection: if rulings are not the subject matter, why are they discussed in usul works? The answer reveals a broader principle about how sciences treat incidental topics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Read the opening phrase noting a dispute over ‘the subject-matter nature of rulings.’ Ask: What exactly is being disputed? The status of aḥkām as part of the mawḍū‘ of usul al-fiqh.
2
Contrast the two positions
Find the view of Sadr al-Shari‘ah and al-Taftazani: rulings are subject matter because they share with evidence the concept of proof (ithbāt). Then compare with the author’s stance: evidence alone is the subject matter; rulings are not.
The common factor is proof, but the author argues that does not make rulings part of the subject matter.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection: if rulings are not subject matter, why are they mentioned in usul works? The author offers two reasons: (a) to define rulings and list their types, and (b) it is common for any science to include extraneous material for completeness.
The second reason is the general principle that sciences may discuss incidental topics (istiṭrādan).
4
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Consider how this dispute affects the definition of usul al-fiqh. If rulings are part of the subject matter, the definition must include them; if not, the definition can focus solely on evidence. This influences the organization of usul works.
The author’s definition of the benefit (fā’idah) of usul is ‘knowledge of shar‘i rulings from their evidences’ – reflecting that rulings are the outcome, not the subject.
5
Reflect on the commentary’s structure
The commentator says the matn’s phrase can be understood in three ways: (1) stating the ikhtilāf, (2) indicating the author’s preferred view, (3) repelling a supposed objection. Notice how the commentary unpacks each.
This shows the commentator’s method of explaining multiple layers of meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وفي موضوعية الأحكام اختلاف ويمكن أن يكون المقصود من هذه العبارة ثلاثة أشياء : الأول : بيان الاختلاف في كون الأحكام موضوعاً لعلم أصول الفقه. والثاني : الإشارة إلى ما هو الحق عند المصنف من عدم موضوعية الأحكام لأصول الفقه، مع أنّ صدر الشريعة في التوضيح والتفتازانى فى التلويح "اختارا كون الأحكام موضوعاً لأصول الفقه مثل الأدلة والقدر المشترك بين الأحكام والأدلة هو الاثبات، فالأدلّة تكون موضوعاً من حيث كونها مثبتة لها، والأحكام من حيث كونها مثبتة بالأدلة. والثالث : دفع اعتراض موهوم يرد على المصنف بأنه لم ترك الأحكام ولم يقل: وموضوعه الأدلة والأحكام؟ وحاصل الدفع أن المصنف بصدد بيان الامور الاتفاقية، وموضوعية الأحكام مختلف فيها. ولما ورد الإشكال بأن الأحكام لما لم تكن موضوعاً لأصول الفقه عند المصنف فلم ذكر الأحكام في كتابه ؟ فأجاب عنه بوجهين: الأول : ليس ذكر بحث الأحكام في أصول الفقه لأجل أنها موضوع علم الأصول، بل ،الغرض من ذكرها هنا تبعاً أمران: الأول: بيان تعريف الأحكام، والثاني بيان أقسامها . والوجه الثاني: أنه ما من علم إلا و يذكر فيه الأشياء الأخر التي ليست منه، وإنما تذكر استطراداً وتبعاً وتتميماً لمعرفة أحوال الموضوع وترميماً (اصلاحاً للغاية المقصودة من ذلك العلم وفائدة علم أصول الفقه (وغايته) وفائدته معرفة الأحكام الشرعية من أدلتها) وهي سبب لفائدته الأساسية) و هو الفوز بالسعادة الأبدية (و لو قال المصنّف : وفائدته معرفة طريق استنباط الأحكام الشرعية لكان أوفق بغاية الفن وغرض وضعه)
His statement: "And there is a difference concerning the subject-matter nature of rulings." And the intended meaning of this expression could be three things: First: explaining the difference of opinion on whether rulings are a subject matter of the science of usul al-fiqh. Second: indicating what according to the author is correct, namely that rulings are not the subject matter of usul al-fiqh, although Sadr al-Shari'ah in al-Tawdih and al-Taftazani in al-Talwih chose that rulings are a subject matter of usul al-fiqh like evidence, and the common factor between rulings and evidence is proof, so evidence is subject matter in terms of being proving them, and rulings in terms of being proven by evidence. Third: repelling a supposed objection against the author that he omitted rulings and did not say: "its subject matter is evidence and rulings"? The summary of the response is that the author is in the context of explaining matters of agreement, and the subject-matter nature of rulings is disputed. When the objection was raised that since rulings are not the subject matter of usul al-fiqh according to the author, why did he mention rulings in his book? He answered it with two points: First: mentioning the discussion of rulings in usul al-fiqh is not because they are the subject matter of the science of usul, but rather the purpose of mentioning them here as a subordinate matter is two things: (1) explaining the definition of rulings, and (2) explaining their divisions. The second point: that there is no science except that other things which are not from it are mentioned in it, and they are mentioned only digressively, as subordinate, to complete the knowledge of the states of the subject matter, and as a correction (repair) for the intended purpose of that science. And the benefit of the science of usul al-fiqh (and its purpose): its benefit is knowledge of the shar'i rulings from their evidences, which is the cause of its primary benefit, which is attaining eternal happiness. (And if the author had said: its benefit is knowledge of the method of deriving shar'i rulings, it would have been more suitable to the goal of the discipline and the purpose for which it was composed.)
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn (likely from Musallam al-Thubut) stating that there is a dispute over whether ahkam (legal rulings) constitute the subject matter of usul al-fiqh. The commentator, al-Badakhshani, explains three objectives of this statement: (1) to highlight the difference of opinion, (2) to clarify the author's position that rulings are not the subject matter, contrary to what Sadr al-Shari'ah and al-Taftazani held, and (3) to preempt an objection: if rulings are not the subject matter, why does the author mention them? The author defends that rulings are discussed incidentally for two reasons: to define them and to enumerate their types. Furthermore, it is common in any science to include extraneous topics for the sake of completeness. The passage concludes by stating the benefit of usul al-fiqh: knowledge of shar'i rulings from their evidences, leading to eternal happiness, though the commentator notes that defining the benefit as knowledge of the method of derivation would be more precise.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍū‘): The subject matter of a science; the primary object of study.
أحكام (aḥkām): Shar'i rulings, the legal qualifications of acts (e.g., wajib, haram).
أدلة (adillah): The sources/evidence from which rulings are derived (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas).
استطراداً (istiṭrādan): In a digressive or incidental manner, not as a primary subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because rulings are the primary subject matter
B) To define rulings and list their types, and because it is common to include extraneous topics for completeness
C) To prove that Sadr al-Shari'ah was wrong
D) Because the matn forced him to

Key Takeaway
Whether rulings are part of usul al-fiqh's subject matter is a legitimate ikhtilāf; the majority Hanafi view restricts the subject to evidence, and rulings are discussed incidentally for definitional and classificatory purposes.

The Need for Theological Principles in Usul al-Fiqh: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining methodological difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i in Usul al-Fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i methods regarding the need for theological and logical principles in Usul al-Fiqh.

Core Idea
Hanafis base Usul on transmitted rules; Shafi'is rely on rational rules, requiring theological principles.

This lesson explores a foundational methodological difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools of Usul al-Fiqh. The text explains why theological principles are necessary in some approaches but not others, depending on whether the school builds its methodology on transmitted or rational rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods
Note that the text contrasts two methods: the Shafi'i method based on rational rules (قواعد المعقول) and the Hanafi method based on transmitted rules (قواعد المنقول).
This is the core distinction.
2
Understand the dependency
The Shafi'i method requires theological principles because rational rules need a theological foundation. The Hanafi method does not require them because transmitted rules are self-sufficient.
Consider how this affects the structure of usul texts.
3
Analyze the author's justification
The author includes theological principles because the text being commented on (Muslim al-Thubut) is a mixture of both methods, so theological preliminaries are needed for completeness.
This is a practical reason, not a doctrinal one.
4
Examine the treatment of logical principles
Logical principles are also needed, but the author omits them because he already covered them in his earlier works (al-Sullam and al-Ifadat), and because later scholars integrated logic into theology.
This shows awareness of audience and existing resources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الأولى في المبادئ الكلامية ومنها المنطقية، لأنهم جعلوه جزءا من الكلام، وقد فرغنا عنها في "السلم" و "الافادات" والآن نذكر طرفاً ضرورياً منها المقالة الأولى في المسائل التي تتعلق بعلم الكلام، ولكن تكون تلك المسائل من مبادئ أصول الفقه ؛ حيث يتوقف عليها فهم بعض مسائل أصول الفقه، وإنما يكون هذا التوقف في الطريقة التى اختارها الشافعية؛ حيث أسسوا أصول الفقه على قواعد المعقول، وأما في الطريقة الحنفية فلا حاجة إلى تلك المبادى؛ لأنهم أسسوا أصول الفقه على قواعد المنقول، وكتب الطرفين تشهد على ما نقول. وبما أن الكتاب الذى نحن بصدد شرحه مخلوط بين الطريقتين احتاج المصنف إلى المبادى الكلامية ثم أشار إلى حاجة أصول الفقه إلى المبادى المنطقية أيضاً، وقال "ومنها المنطقية" ثم بأنّ المتأخرين جعلوا المنطق جزءا من علم الكلام كما صرح السيد في شرح صرح المواقف، فنسبة المنطق إلى الكلام أقوى، فالمبادى الكلامية تغني عن ذكر المبادى المنطقية. وأشار إلى الوجه الثاني لعدم ذكر المبادى المنطقية هنا بقوله : وقد فرغنا عن ذكرها في كتابنا "السلم" وكتابنا الافادات، فان كليهما في المنطق وقواعده، ومن احتاج إلى المبادى المنطقية فليرجع إليهما . قال الشيخ المارتونكى : " ومنها أي من المبادى الكلامية المبادى المنطقية، ولما ورد الإشكال بأن المصنف لماذا لم يذكر المبادى المنطقية مع أن الأصولى يحتاج اليها؛ فأجاب
The first treatise on theological principles, and among them logical principles, because they made it a part of kalam, and we have already dealt with them in "al-Sullam" and "al-Ifadat", and now we mention a necessary part of them. The first treatise: on matters related to the science of kalam, but these matters are among the principles of usul al-fiqh, because understanding some usul al-fiqh issues depends on them. And this dependence is only in the method chosen by the Shafi'is, who founded usul al-fiqh on rational rules; as for the Hanafi method, there is no need for those principles because they founded usul al-fiqh on transmitted rules. The books of both parties testify to what we say. And since the book we are commenting on is a mixture of both methods, the author needed theological principles. Then he indicated the need of usul al-fiqh for logical principles as well, saying 'and among them logical principles.' Then he explained that later scholars made logic a part of the science of kalam, as al-Sayyid clarified in his commentary on Sharh al-Mawaqif, so the relationship of logic to kalam is stronger, thus theological principles suffice to mention logical principles. And he pointed to the second aspect for not mentioning logical principles here by saying: 'We have already dealt with them in our book "al-Sullam" and our book "al-Ifadat", for both are on logic and its rules. Whoever needs logical principles should refer to them.' Al-Shaykh al-Martūnki said: 'Among them, meaning from the theological principles, logical principles.' And since the objection arose: Why did the author not mention logical principles, despite the usuli needing them; he answered...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage begins the first treatise of the book. The commentator explains the need for theological and logical principles in Usul al-Fiqh. He distinguishes between the Shafi'i method, which depends on rational principles, and the Hanafi method, which relies on transmitted principles. Because the text being commented on (Muslim al-Thubut) is a mixture of both, the author includes theological principles. Logical principles are also needed, but the author omitted them because he already covered them in his earlier works (al-Sullam and al-Ifadat). The commentator also notes that later scholars integrated logic into theology, so theological principles suffice. This is an important methodological clarification for students.

Key Terms
المبادئ الكلامية (al-mabādiʼ al-kalāmiyya): Theological principles that are foundational for some usul al-fiqh issues, especially in the Shafi'i method.
قواعد المعقول (qawāʻid al-maʻqūl): Rational rules upon which Shafi'is base usul al-fiqh.
قواعد المنقول (qawāʻid al-manqūl): Transmitted rules from Quran, Sunnah, and scholarly consensus upon which Hanafis base usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which school of usul al-fiqh requires theological principles as foundational?
A) Hanafi
B) Shafi'i
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Hanafis base Usul al-Fiqh on transmitted rules and do not need theological principles, unlike Shafi'is who rely on rational rules.

Definition of Nazar and Its Role in Usul al-Fiqh
Defining nazar and explaining the inclusion of logical preliminaries in a combined work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nazar according to the Hanafi usul tradition and explain why Hanafi usul does not require extensive logical preliminaries.

Core Idea
Nazar is the arrangement of known premises to reach unknown conclusions — it is the engine of usul al-fiqh.

This passage from the sharh of Musallam al-Thubut introduces the first principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) of logical preliminaries: the definition of nazar. The commentator explains why logical preliminaries are included even though the Hanafi method relies on transmitted sources. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping how rulings are derived through reasoning (istidlal) in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
1. Distinguish linguistic and technical meanings of nazar
The text gives the linguistic meaning (turning the face or heart towards something) and the technical meaning (arrangement of knowns to obtain unknowns). Recognize that the technical meaning is the one used in usul.
The linguistic meaning is not irrelevant, but the technical definition is the operational one.
2
2. Identify the two core components: al-ma'qul and al-majhul
Al-ma'qul (the known) refers to conceptual or affirmational information (second intelligibles). Al-majhul (the unknown) is what we seek through reasoning. Note that both can be few or many.
The lam in both words indicates genus, so they include all possibilities.
3
3. Understand the condition of multiple knowns
Arrangement (tartib) is only possible with at least two knowns. This means nazar requires at least two premises to function.
This condition is implied by the word 'tartib' which means ordering multiple items.
4
4. Relate nazar to the Hanafi method
The text states that Hanafi usul does not need extensive logical preliminaries because it is based on transmitted (manqul) rules, unlike the Shafi'i method which relies on rational (ma'qul) rules. This explains why the author, in a mixed work, still includes these preliminaries.
Later Hanafis did adopt some logical tools, but the core method remains text-based.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الأولى
في المبادئ الكلامية
ومنها المنطقية، لأنهم جعلوه جزءا من الكلام، وقد فرغنا عنها في "السلم" و "الافادات" والآن نذكر طرفاً ضرورياً منها
المقالة الأولى في المسائل التي تتعلق بعلم الكلام، ولكن تكون تلك المسائل من مبادئ أصول الفقه ؛ حيث يتوقف عليها فهم بعض مسائل أصول الفقه، وإنما يكون هذا التوقف في الطريقة التى اختارها الشافعية؛ حيث أسسوا أصول الفقه على قواعد المعقول، وأما في الطريقة الحنفية فلا حاجة إلى تلك المبادى؛ لأنهم أسسوا أصول الفقه على قواعد المنقول، وكتب الطرفين تشهد على ما نقول. وبما أن الكتاب الذى نحن بصدد شرحه مخلوط بين الطريقتين احتاج المصنف إلى
المبادى الكلامية
ثم أشار إلى حاجة أصول الفقه إلى المبادى المنطقية أيضاً، وقال "ومنها المنطقية" ثم بأنّ المتأخرين جعلوا المنطق جزءا من علم الكلام كما صرح السيد في شرح صرح المواقف، فنسبة المنطق إلى الكلام أقوى، فالمبادى الكلامية تغني عن ذكر المبادى المنطقية. وأشار إلى الوجه الثاني لعدم ذكر المبادى المنطقية هنا بقوله : وقد فرغنا عن ذكرها في كتابنا ”السلم" وكتابنا الافادات، فان كليهما في المنطق وقواعده، ومن احتاج إلى المبادى المنطقية فليرجع إليهما . قال الشيخ المارتونكى : " ومنها أي من المبادى الكلامية المبادى المنطقية، ولما ورد الإشكال بأن المصنف لماذا لم يذكر المبادى المنطقية مع أن الأصولى يحتاج اليها؛ فأجاب
عنه بقوله : لأنهم جعلوه جزءا من الكلام، وحاصل الجواب أن ذكر المبادى الكلامية يستلزم ذكر المبادى المنطقية ؛ لأن المتأخرين جعلوا المنطق جزءا من الكلام، فذكر
مباديه ذكر مباديه.
فائدة : وإنما جعل المنطق جزءا من الكلام ؛ لأن المنطق هو علم بطرق الحدود
،والبرهان ويحتاج كل مستدل اليهما، إذ الدليل والبرهان يحتاج إلى تصور الأشياء وحدودها اوّلاً وإلى التصديق بأحكامها وأحوالها ثانياً، والكلام علم بالدلائل
القطعية التي تفيد معرفة الذات والصفات الالهية التي يمتاز بها عن خلقه، وكذلك تفيد معرفة حقية المعاد، والرسالة والمعجزات اللازمة لها ، وغيرها مما يجب به الايمان، وبما أن مدار علم الكلام على الدلائل العقلية التي اعتمد عليها الفلاسفة والمعتزلة وغيرهما من الفرق العقلانية البحتة، وجل مساعى المتكلمين وعلماء أهل السنة هو الدفاع عن تلك الدلائل بوجوه عقلية مسلّمة مبنية على قواعد المنطق وأصوله، احتاج المتكلمون وعلماء الكلام إلى مبادي المنطق وأصوله وقواعده، فجعلوه جزءا للكلام جزئية مبادية لا أساسية وأصلية .

الجانب الضرورى من المبادى المنطقية
-١ المبدأ الأوّل: تعريف النظر وحكمه : وهو في اللغة إما التوجه إلى الشيئ بعين الوجه ، وإمّا التوجه إليه بعين القلب، وهذا هو المراد هنا وفي الاصطلاح :) هو ترتيب المعقول (المعلومات التصورية أو التصديقة التي تكون من المعقولات الثانية) لتحصيل المجهول (أي لتحصيل التصور المجهول أو التصديق المجهول)
واللام في (المعقول للجنس فيشمل القليل والكثير ، وإنما يتحقق الترتيب في الاثنين فصاعداً، فيراد بالمعقول الكثير، وكذلك اللام في (المجهول) للجنس، فإنّ المجهول الذى يُحصل قد يكون قليلاً وقد يكون كثيراً، فيشمل المجهول التصوري
The first article on theological preliminaries. And among them are logical (preliminaries), because they made it a part of kalam, and we have already completed them in 'al-Sullam' and 'al-Ifadat', and now we mention a necessary portion of them. The first article deals with issues related to the science of kalam, but these issues are among the principles of usul al-fiqh, because understanding some matters of usul al-fiqh depends on them. This dependence exists only in the method chosen by the Shafi'is, who founded usul al-fiqh on rational rules. As for the Hanafi method, there is no need for those principles, because they founded usul al-fiqh on transmitted rules, and the books of both sides testify to what we say. Since the book we are explaining is a mixture of both methods, the author needed the theological principles. Then he pointed to the need of usul al-fiqh for logical principles as well, saying 'and among them are the logical (principles).' Then (he explained) that the later scholars made logic a part of the science of kalam, as al-Sayyid clarified in his commentary on Sharh al-Mawaqif, so the relation of logic to kalam is stronger. Thus theological principles suffice for mentioning logical principles. He pointed to the second reason for not mentioning logical principles here by saying: 'We have already completed them in our book al-Sullam and our book al-Ifadat, for both are on logic and its rules, and whoever needs logical principles should refer to them.' Shaykh al-Martunki said: 'And among them, i.e., among the theological principles, are the logical principles.' When the objection arose that why did the author not mention logical principles while the usuli needs them, he answered by saying: 'Because they made it a part of kalam.' The gist of the answer is that mentioning theological principles entails mentioning logical principles, because the later scholars made logic a part of kalam, so mentioning its principles is mentioning its principles. Benefit: Logic was made a part of kalam only because logic is the science of the methods of definitions and proofs, and every reasoner needs them both, since evidence and proof require conceptualizing things and their definitions first, and then affirming their judgments and states second. Kalam is the science of definitive proofs that lead to knowledge of the Essence and Attributes of God by which He is distinguished from His creation, and also lead to knowledge of the truth of the resurrection, prophethood, and the miracles necessary for it, and other things that require faith. Since the foundation of kalam is on rational proofs relied upon by philosophers, Mu'tazila, and other purely rationalist sects, and most of the efforts of the mutakallimin and Sunni scholars are to defend those proofs by accepted rational methods based on the rules and principles of logic, the mutakallimin and scholars of kalam needed the principles, rules, and foundations of logic, so they made it a part of kalam as a subsidiary part, not as a fundamental one. The necessary aspect of logical principles: First principle: Definition of nazar and its ruling: In language it is either turning the face toward something or turning the heart toward it, and that is what is meant here. In terminology: It is the arrangement of the known (conceptual or affirmational information that are second intelligibles) to obtain the unknown (i.e., to obtain the unknown concept or the unknown affirmation). The lam in 'al-ma'qul' is for the genus, so it includes few and many, and arrangement is only realized in two or more, so what is meant is the many known things. Similarly, the lam in 'al-majhul' is for the genus, for the unknown that is obtained may be little or much, so it includes conceptual unknown.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary (sharh) on the matn 'Musallam al-Thubut' by al-Badakhshani. It explains the author's approach to including theological and logical preliminaries in a work of usul al-fiqh. The author notes that while the Hanafi school does not require such preliminaries (since it is based on transmitted texts), the Shafi'i method does, and because this book combines both methods, these preliminaries are included. The commentary then explains why logical principles are subsumed under theological ones: later scholars made logic part of kalam. The benefit (fa'ida) elaborates on the relationship between logic and kalam. Finally, the first principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) defines nazar (consideration/reasoning) both linguistically and technically. The technical definition: 'the arrangement of known concepts to obtain what is unknown.' The explanation clarifies that 'known' (ma'qul) includes multiple items, and 'unknown' (majhul) can be a concept or an affirmation. This definition is foundational for usul al-fiqh, as reasoning (nazar) is the method by which rulings are derived.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar): Arranging known concepts (second intelligibles) to obtain unknown knowledge.
المعقول (al-ma'qul): Known conceptual or affirmational information, specifically second intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya).
المجهول (al-majhul): The unknown concept or affirmation sought through reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the technical definition of nazar (النظر)?
A) The study of logical proofs.
B) The arrangement of known concepts to obtain unknown knowledge.
C) Turning the heart toward something.
D) The science of definitions and proofs.

Key Takeaway
Nazar, the arrangement of knowns, is fundamental to usul al-fiqh, but the Hanafi school does not require formal logical preliminaries.

Definition and Its Conditions; Three Objections in Debate
Explaining definition, its conditions, and three objections in debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of a valid definition (tard and 'aks) according to early Hanafis, distinguish between the positions of early and late scholars, and identify the three types of objections (al-man', al-naqd, al-mu'arada) that can be applied to a definition via its implicit claims.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be both comprehensive (tard) and exclusive ('aks); objections target its implicit claims, not the definition itself.

This passage from 'Izalat al-Rahbūt' explains the concept of definition in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It lays out the two key conditions of a proper definition and a significant internal debate between early and later scholars. It then clarifies how objections apply not to definitions directly but to the underlying claims they contain, and categorizes the three standard objections used in debate: denial (man'), refutation (naqd), and counter-argument (mu'arada). Understanding these distinctions is essential for engaging in scholarly discussion (munazara) and correctly formulating legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions of a proper definition
The passage states that a definition (al-mu'arraf bi al-kasr) must have tard (comprehensiveness, preventing entry of non-members) and 'aks (exclusiveness, encompassing all members). These are necessary conditions for validity according to early Hanafis.
Tard prevents what is outside from entering; 'aks ensures all inside are included.
2
Distinguish early vs. late Hanafi positions
Early scholars require equality (musawah) between definer and defined, so tard and 'aks are necessary. Later scholars allow definition by a broader term (al-a'amm), so they do not require tard and 'aks. Understand the practical consequence (thamarah).
Later scholars accept 'human is an animal' as a valid definition; early scholars insist on 'rational animal'.
3
Grasp that objections target implicit claims, not the definition itself
Definitions are pure depictions (taswir) without explicit rulings. However, they carry implicit claims (da'awa dhimniyya) such as being a proper definition (haddiyya), description (rusmiyya), being comprehensive (ittirād), and being exclusive (in'ikās). It is these claims that can be objected to.
The three objections (man', naqd, mu'arada) do not apply directly to the wording but to these accompanying claims.
4
Categorize the three types of objections
1) al-Man': a request for proof from the claimant. 2) al-Naqd: refutation—either general (rejecting the whole claim) or detailed (rejecting each part and evidence). 3) al-Mu'arada: counter-argument, providing a proof opposite to the claimant's. These belong to the science of debate (munazara) and disagreement (khilaf), not logic.
Memorize the definitions: man' asks for evidence, naqd invalidates, mu'arada counters with opposite proof.
5
Apply to a Hanafi usul example
Consider the definition of 'salah': 'acts of worship beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. A challenger might object via man' (ask for proof of comprehensiveness/exclusiveness), naqd (point out it excludes certain invalid prayers), or mu'arada (offer a different definition). Recognize that the objection is to the implicit claim of being a proper definition (hadd), not to the act of defining itself.
This shows the practical application of the lesson in scholarly debate.

The fourth principle: On explaining the definition and its types. The definition (al-mu'arraf) is what prevents the internal [element] from leaving the individuals of the defined, and the external from entering. So it is necessary in the definition (with kasra) to have comprehensiveness (tard)—which is being a preventer of the entry of others into it—and exclusiveness ('aks)—which is being inclusive of all its individuals—meaning it excludes the non-defined and includes (applies to) the defined. The definition (with kasra) is what provides a conception of something in some way. Its condition is that it prevents the entry of the non-defined (with fatha) into the definition and encompasses all individuals of the defined (with fatha). This is according to the early scholars who require equality between the definer (with kasra) and the defined (with fatha). As for the later scholars, they permit definition by the more general, so comprehensiveness and exclusiveness are not required for them. The objections to the definition only arise due to implicit claims. All objections (that come) to the definition are claims, and denial (man') suffices in response. Know that the three objections (denial, refutation, and counter-argument) only apply to speech that contains a ruling. As for the definition whose purpose is to depict and explain the defined, none of the three objections apply to it. Yes, it contains rulings and implicit claims, such as the claim of being a definition (haddiyyah), being a description (rusmiyyah), being comprehensive (itṭirād), and being exclusive (in‘ikās). So one of the three objections applies to the definition with respect to those implicit claims and rulings. For this, the author said: 'All objections to the definition are claims,' meaning it includes implicit claims and rulings. So no problem arises that the definition is pure depiction without ruling, so how can the three objections apply? The three objections: (1) Denial (al-man') is asking the claimant for proof of his claim. (2) Refutation (al-naqd) is of two types: general—denying the claim generally by saying 'your claim is false because of such and such'; and detailed—the objector says 'your claim is false in all its parts and proofs.' (3) Counter-argument (al-mu‘āraḍa) is providing proof contrary to what the claimant brought. These three objections are terms of the science of debate and disagreement, not terms of logic. For the scholars of...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) explaining the concept of definition (al-mu'arraf) and its types. The author first defines definition as having two essential conditions: tard (comprehensiveness, preventing entry of what is not included) and 'aks (exclusiveness, encompassing all individuals). He notes a difference of opinion: early scholars insist on equality between definer and defined (so tard and 'aks are required), while later scholars allow definition by a broader term, thus not requiring tard and 'aks. The author then addresses objections (iradat) to definitions. He clarifies that objections do not target the definition itself as a pure depiction, but rather target the implicit claims that accompany a definition—such as the claim that it is a proper definition (hadd) or description (rasm) or that it is comprehensive and exclusive. The three types of objections are: (1) al-man': denial or asking for proof; (2) al-naqd: refutation, which can be general (invalidating the whole claim) or detailed (invalidating each part); (3) al-mu'arada: counter-argument, producing a contrary proof. These terms belong to the science of debate (munazara) and disagreement (khilaf), not logic. In Hanafi usul, understanding these is crucial for proper legal debate and definition in fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
المعرف بالكسر (al-mu'arraf bi'l-kasr): The definition (definiens) that provides conceptualization of something.
الطرد (al-tard): Comprehensiveness: the definition prevents the entry of what is not part of the defined.
العكس (al-'aks): Exclusiveness: the definition encompasses all individuals of the defined.
المنع (al-man'): An objection that demands proof from the claimant; it is a type of iradah.
النقص (al-naqd): Refutation: an objection that invalidates the claim, either generally or in detail.
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Counter-argument: providing a proof contrary to the claimant's proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A valid definition requires tard and 'aks according to early Hanafis; later Hanafis allow broader definitions; objections apply only to implicit claims, not the definition itself.

Types of Definition and the Rule of Definition by the More General
Explains types of definition and the rule of definition by the more general

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of definition (essential, descriptive, verbal) and apply the Hanafi rule that definition by the more general is permitted only in essential definition.

Core Idea
Definition by the more general is only permitted in essential definition, not verbal, because verbal definition requires synonymy.

Definitions are foundational to legal reasoning in Usul al-Fiqh. This passage categorises definitions into three types based on the kind of properties used: essential (dhatiyyat), descriptive (lawazim), or synonymous expression. It also clarifies a key Hanafi rule: when an essential definition aims only to partially distinguish the definiendum, it may use a more general term. However, this permission does not extend to verbal definitions, which require exact synonymy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of definition
List the three types: essential (haqiqi), descriptive (rasmi), and verbal (lafzi). Note the basis for each: essential uses essential properties (dhatiyyat), descriptive uses necessary concomitants (lawazim), and verbal uses a more apparent synonym.
The passage gives classic examples: human = rational animal (essential), human = laughing animal (descriptive), ghadanfar = lion (verbal).
2
Understand the rule of definition by the more general
The early Hanafi scholars permitted essential definition by a more general term when the goal is merely to distinguish the definiendum from some, not all, other things. Example: defining human as 'animal' alone.
This rule applies only to essential definition, not to descriptive or verbal.
3
Recognize the mistake of misapplying the rule to verbal definition
The commentator criticizes someone who applied the permission to verbal definition. Verbal definition requires a synonym (same meaning), but a more general term has a broader meaning, not identical. Therefore, it cannot be a verbal definition.
Generality and specificity are attributes of meaning, not of the word itself. A synonym cannot be more general in meaning.
4
Distinguish between the purposes of definition
Essential definitions can aim for full conceptualization or partial distinction. The rule allows partial distinction using a more general term. In contrast, verbal definitions aim to clarify the term by a more familiar synonym, which must be exactly equivalent in meaning.
The early Hanafis did not require equality (musawat) between definiens and definiendum in essential definitions when the goal is partial distinction.
5
Apply the rule to other examples
Consider defining 'word' as 'utterance' (lafz). Here 'word' (kalima) is a specific type of utterance; using 'utterance' alone is a definition by the more general. This is permitted if the purpose is partial distinction.
This example appears in the passage: defining kalima as lafz.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Debate and argumentation use them in their scientific debates and arguments, and they seek help from them in confronting their opponents. Then the author said: And it suffices in answering them, answering those objections by denial, i.e., it suffices in answering each one of those objections when raised against the definition that the definer says: Your objection is prevented and false, so I do not accept it. The types of the defined: It is of three types: the essential, the descriptive, and the verbal. It is essential if it is by essential properties, descriptive if by necessary concomitants, and verbal if by a more apparent and synonymous expression for the defined. 1. The essential definition is that which is by essential properties, like defining human as rational animal. 2. The descriptive definition is that which is by necessary concomitants, like defining him as laughing animal or writing animal. 3. The verbal definition is that which is by an expression more apparent and clearer in indication to the concept than the defined, and synonymous to the defined, like defining al-ghadanfar as lion or al-‘uqar as wine. And the author indicated the doctrine of the early scholars regarding not requiring equality between the definition and the defined, and said: And definition by the more general has been permitted, i.e., permission has been given for the essential definition by the more general, when the purpose of the essential definition is to distinguish the defined from some of what is other than it, without conceptualizing its essence or distinguishing it from all what is other than it, like defining human by animal only or defining word by utterance only. And among the wonders is the statement of one who explained the author's phrase by saying: The verbal definition by the more general has been permitted, because the verbal definition is by another expression more famous and clearer than the expression of the defined, and generality and specificity are attributes of meaning, so how can the more famous expression be more general or more specific than the expression of the defined? Also, the expression occurring in the verbal definition is synonymous with the expression of the defined, so their meaning is one, whereas the general is not the specific.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's text regarding definitions. It begins by describing how definitions are used in debates to counter opponents. Then it explains that when objections are raised against a definition, the definer can simply deny the objection by saying it is prevented (mamnu') and false. Next, it outlines the three types of definitions: essential (haqiqi), descriptive (rasmi), and verbal (lafzi). Essential definition uses essential properties (dhatiyyat) like defining human as 'rational animal'. Descriptive definition uses necessary concomitants (lawazim) like defining human as 'laughing animal'. Verbal definition uses a more apparent synonym, e.g., defining 'ghadanfar' as 'lion'. The author then points out that the early scholars did not require equality between definiens and definiendum; they permitted essential definition by a more general term when the goal is merely to distinguish the definiendum from some others, not to fully conceptualize its essence. For example, defining human as 'animal' alone is permissible. The commentator then criticizes someone who mistakenly applied this permission to verbal definition, arguing that verbal definition requires synonymy, so the term cannot be more general in meaning. This clarifies that the permission for definition by the more general applies only to essential definition, not verbal.

Key Terms
التعريف الحقيقى (al-ta‘rīf al-ḥaqīqī): Definition that uses essential properties (al-dhātiyyāt) of the definiendum.
التعريف الرسمى (al-ta‘rīf al-rasmī): Definition that uses necessary concomitants (al-lawāzim) of the definiendum.
التعريف اللفظى (al-ta‘rīf al-lafẓī): Definition that uses a more apparent synonymous expression for the definiendum.
التعريف بالأعم (al-ta‘rīf bi’l-a‘amm): Defining a term by a more general term, permitted only in essential definition when the goal is partial distinction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Definition by the more general is a Hanafi permission that applies only to essential definitions when the goal is partial distinction, not to verbal definitions which require exact synonymy.

Types of Definitions in Usul al-Fiqh
Presenting subdivisions of essential and descriptive definitions and the disagreement on nominal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between the four types of definition (perfect/imperfect essential and descriptive) and explain the status of nominal definition according to different usul scholars.

Core Idea
Definitions are essential (by genus/differentia) or descriptive (by accidents), each perfect or imperfect, with nominal definition as a disputed fifth type.

This lesson clarifies the subdivisions of definition used in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh. Essential definitions capture the quiddity of a thing, while descriptive definitions rely on accidentals. Additionally, there is a fifth type—nominal definition—whose classification is disputed among scholars. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precisely defining legal concepts like 'sale' or 'fasting'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two broad categories
Recognize that essential definition (al-taʿrīf al-ḥaqīqī) defines a thing by its essence, while descriptive definition (al-taʿrīf al-rasmī) defines it by accidents.
2
Note the perfect/imperfect subtypes
Each category has perfect (tāmm) and imperfect (nāqiṣ) versions: perfect includes both genus and differentia (or proper accident and its differentia), imperfect includes only one.
3
Understand the perfect essential definition
Example: 'Human is a rational animal' (genus + differentia). This is al-ḥadd al-tāmm.
Imperfect essential: 'Human is an animal' (only genus).
4
Recognize nominal definition
Nominal definition (al-taʿrīf al-ismī) defines conceptual entities like 'legal ruling' by their conceptual components. It is a fifth type according to some scholars.
Controversy: logicians include it in essential definition; al-Tawdih treats it as separate.
5
Compare conflicting views
Note that the author of al-Tawdih considers nominal definition as a counterpart to essential, while ʿAbd al-Ḥaqq al-Khayr Ābādī says logicians include it within essential.
Both views are used in Hanafi usul, so students should be aware of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة التعريف الحقيقى على نوعين : حدّ تام وحد ناقص، وكذلك التعريف الرسمى له قسمان: رسم تام ورسم ناقص، فصار المجموع أربعة وفي التوضيح: التعريف إمّا ،حقيقي، كتعريف الماهيات الحقيقة، وإما اسمى كتعريف الماهيات الاعتبارية، وقد ذكرنا أمثلتها (أمثلة الماهيات الاعتبارية سابقاً، ويفهم من كلام صاحب التوضيح أن هنا قسماً خامساً للتعريف لم يذكره المصنف، وهو التعريف الاسمي وقال عبد الحق الخير آبادى في شرحه على المسلّم" ص ۳۹: فالتعريف الاسمى عندهم داخل في التعريف الحقيقى أي جعل المنطقيون التعريف الاسمى نوعاً من التعريف الحقيقى، ولكن صاحب التوضيح “ جعل التعريف الاسمى قسيماً ومقابلاً للتعريف الحقيقي. الذاتي وتعريفه والذاتي: ١- ما يكون) فهمه داخلاً في فهم الذات، ٢- وقيل (هو) ما لا يعلّل ما لا يذكر لثبوته للذات علّة ونُقِضَ (التعريف الثاني بصفة) الإمكان ؛ فإنه يصدق عليه أنه ثابت للمكن من غير علّة، إذ لا إمكان بالغير أي) لا يكون وجود صفة الإمكان في الممكن لأجل أن صفة الإمكان ليس بذاتى للمكن، بل من عوارضه غيره مع فدخل العرضى في تعريف الذاتى ) وبعد ما قال المصنف : " هو (التعريف ) حقيقي إن كان بالذاتيات “ أراد أن يعرف الذاتى فقال : والذاتى الخ. ثم ذكر للذاتي تعريفين : وأشار إلى ضعف الثاني بقوله: "ونقض بالإمكان“، فحاصل التعريف الأول: أن الذاتي هو الذى يستلزم فهم ذات الشئ فهمه، بحيث تدل الذات نفسها على الذاتى، فكلّما عُلِمت ذات الشئ عُلم ذاتيه.
Benefit: The essential definition is of two types: perfect definition and imperfect definition. Similarly, the descriptive definition has two divisions: perfect descriptional definition and imperfect descriptional definition. So the total becomes four. And in al-Tawdih: Definition is either essential, like defining real essences, or nominal, like defining conceptual entities. And we have mentioned their examples (examples of conceptual entities previously). And it is understood from the words of the author of al-Tawdih that there is a fifth division of definition that the author did not mention, which is the nominal definition. And ‘Abd al-Haqq al-Khayr Abadi said in his commentary on al-Muslim: The nominal definition according to them is included in the essential definition, i.e., the logicians made the nominal definition a type of essential definition, but the author of al-Tawdih made the nominal definition a counterpart and opposite to the essential definition. The essential (al-dhati) and its definition. Al-Dhati: 1. That whose understanding is included in the understanding of the essence. 2. And it is said it is that which is not caused, i.e., no cause is mentioned for its being established for the essence. And the second definition is refuted by the attribute of possibility (imkan), for it is true of it that it is established for the possible without a cause, since there is no possibility by another, i.e., the existence of the attribute of possibility in the possible is not because the attribute of possibility is essential for the possible, but rather it is among its accidental attributes, so the accidental enters into the definition of the essential. And after the author said: "It (the definition) is essential if by essential properties," he wanted to define the essential, so he said: al-dhati, etc. Then he mentioned two definitions for al-dhati, and he indicated the weakness of the second by saying: "and it is refuted by possibility." So the summary of the first definition is that al-dhati is that which understanding of the essence of a thing necessitates understanding it, such that the essence itself indicates the essential, so whenever the essence of a thing is known, its essential is known.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on definitions from the author, providing further subdivisions and a detailed analysis of 'al-dhati' (the essential). It begins with a 'faa'idah' (benefit) stating that essential definitions (al-ta'rīf al-ḥaqīqī) are divided into perfect (tāmm) and imperfect (nāqiṣ) definitions, and descriptive definitions (al-ta'rīf al-rasmī) are similarly divided into perfect and imperfect descriptional definitions, making four total types. Then it notes that in al-Tawdih (a commentary on al-Tanqih), a fifth type called 'nominal definition' (al-ta'rīf al-ismī) is introduced for conceptual entities (such as legal rulings). The author quotes 'Abd al-Haqq al-Khayr Abadi, who states that logicians consider nominal definition as a type of essential definition, whereas the author of al-Tawdih treats it as a separate category. The passage then moves to defining 'al-dhati' (essential property). The author presents two definitions: (1) that whose understanding is included in the understanding of the essence (e.g., when you understand 'human', you understand 'animal' and 'rational'), and (2) that which is not caused (i.e., no cause is needed for its existence in the essence). The second definition is criticized by using the example of 'possibility' (imkan): possibility is an attribute that exists in possible beings without a cause, yet it is not essential but accidental. Thus, the second definition fails because it would wrongly include accidental attributes. The first definition is upheld: the essential is what is necessarily understood when the essence is known.

Key Terms
الحد التام (al-ḥadd al-tāmm): A perfect essential definition that includes the genus and differentia.
الحد الناقص (al-ḥadd al-nāqiṣ): An imperfect essential definition that only includes the genus or differentia, not both.
الرسم التام (al-rasm al-tāmm): A perfect descriptional definition that includes a proper accident and its differentia.
الرسم الناقص (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ): An imperfect descriptional definition that includes only a proper accident or its differentia.
التعريف الاسمي (al-ta‘rīf al-ismī): Definition of a nominal/conceptual entity (like legal rulings) by its conceptual components.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four types of definition initially listed?
A) Perfect essential definition (ḥadd tāmm)
B) Imperfect essential definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ)
C) Perfect descriptive definition (rasm tāmm)
D) Nominal definition (taʿrīf ismī)

Key Takeaway
Definitions in usul are classified into essential and descriptive, each perfect and imperfect, with nominal definition as a contentious fifth type.

The Meaning of al-Dhati (Essential) and its Correct Definition
Defining al-dhati and critiquing the second definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct definition of al-dhati and identify why the alternative definition (absence of cause) is flawed.

Core Idea
The essential (al-dhati) is that which is necessarily understood when the essence is known, not merely what exists without a cause.

After discussing types of definitions, the author turns to define 'al-dhati' (the essential), which is the building block of essential definitions. Two definitions are presented: one based on implication in understanding, and another based on absence of cause. The second is refuted using the attribute of possibility (imkan), which shows that absence of cause can apply to accidentals. This lesson dissects the correct definition and the logical critique.

Guided Path
1
Recall the context
The author defines al-dhati because it is the basis of essential definitions (ḥaqīqī). Essential definitions use al-dhati properties.
2
Read the first definition
Al-dhati is that whose understanding is included in the understanding of the essence. Example: when you understand 'human', you understand 'animal' and 'rational'.
This is the accepted definition.
3
Read the second definition
Al-dhati is that which has no cause for its existence in the essence, i.e., it is not caused by something external.
4
Examine the refutation
The attribute of possibility (imkan) exists in possible beings without a cause, yet it is accidental, not essential. Therefore, the second definition would wrongly include imkan as dhati.
Possibility is a property of possible beings, but it is not part of their essence; it is a necessary accident.
5
Conclude
The first definition is sound because it captures the idea that dhati is intrinsic and known from the essence itself. The second is insufficient.
